“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinct ire Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government;.
One Person, one Vote.
Gomolete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prised
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World;
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinamah, the cheap nigger, and the
bheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to bh a
Statemonopoly,
fhe elec: ■< of. M inisters i>y Parlia-
ment, instead uf Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction;
0 ‘\v Parliamentary System -‘one House to!
be , I cted by constituencies as at present;
t,;c Other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A I niVer 1 System Of Compulsory Life
Insnrat:' e.
The entile Abolition of the Private Owner-
, ship of ! . oid.
The Referendum.
J he Abolition of Titles of sO-oalled “nobility- *
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
I he Bulletin is all aggressive; Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti ■
bally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started R) years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
Jhe Bulletin’s! conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparin'*
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the, date of The Bulletins inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. 1 hey are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local .ml dis. inotly universal Australasian news-
paper.
Ihe Bulletin proprietary have spent somf
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced , the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the cla’ins of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any’postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently reunified from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most;
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

SST NOW PUBLISHED,
“ The Last of Six.”


MORE LIKELY TO BREAK DOWN.
Who hasn’t heard of the good old Deacon in
America, and his idea of how a chaise should be
built so as to run for ever without repairs? I dare
say we all have* yet it hasn’t quite gone stale yet.
He said, you remember, that the reason chaises
broke,down and didn’t wear out was that there
was always a weakest spot somewhere about them.
Now, saitl the Deacon, the way to fix it is to make
that spot as strong as the rest. Then the Vehicle
might wear out, but couldn’t break down. He
built a chaise on that principle; it never broke
down; it ran a hundred years exactly, and then
one day it went to pieces all at once ; all at once
and nothing first, just as bubbles do when they
burst. Its time was come ; for, as the man says
who once told the story in rhyme, “ little of all
We value here, wakes on the morn of its hundredth
year, without both feeling and looking queer.”
Yes, and long before that time most of us begin
to look and feel queer. And it’s all on account of
that weak spot, too. If it wasn’t for that wo
should be like the Deacon's chaise we should run
till we wear out. As it is we break down on the
toad, often beyond repair. But not always.
Otherwise a certain man could never have used
these words, “I Jeel as smart to-day as I did
thirty years ago." His story, in his own words,
runs this way :
“From my youth I was never properly well.
1 had a bad taste in the mouth and pain after eat-
ing. Often I couldn’t touch food when it was set
before me. I felt a gnawing at tile stomach and a
tightness at the chest and sides. Sometimes my
bowels were so swollen I had to loose my clothes.
I had Violent pains in my head for days together.
This continued for years and years. About thirty
years ago I began to have rheumatic pains all over
me, especially in the back and legs. I got so bad
1 couldn’t walk without a stick, and on my way to
and from my work I had to sit down and rest.
“I got no proper sleep at night owing to the
pain. Day nor night, I never knew what it was
to bo free from pain. As time went by I became
quite crippled. Doctor after doctor gave me
medicines and rubbing bottles, but none of the
things I tried did any good. The doctors said my
ailment was rheumatism and lumbago and made
light of it, but it was serious enough to me. For
thirty years I suffered dreadfully. My wife used
to rub me before the fire night after night and
apply flannels, relieving me only for the time. I
gave up all hope of ever being well again, when
in November, 1887, I read of the good Mother
Seigel’s Syrup had done in so many cases. I
began to use it, and after taking a few bottles all
my aches and pains left me and have never re-
turned since. This medicine seems to have driven
all the poison out of my system, and I feel as
smart to-day as I did thirty years ago, for which
I thank God and Seigel’s Syrup. Had I used it
sooner I should have been saved years of suffer-
ing.” (Signed) William Stoddart, Douglas,
near Cork, Ireland, January 7th, 1892.
Mr. Stoddart is gardener at Mrs Ed wards’, the
Island, Roehestown, and has a shop at Douglas.
He is a very respectable man and has been in
service at Mrs. Edwards’ twenty years.
In his letter he further states that from boy-
hood he was a victim to indigestion and dyspepsia.
This fact explains the rheumatism, as it was the
cause of it. The bile acids from the torpid
stomach and liver filled the blood, lodged in the
joints and muscles, and gave ri<e to all the con-
sequences he describes. The effect of the Syrup
was to expel the acids from the body and set the
digestive machinery healthily at work, for the
first time in this man’s life.
This was the way the human vehicle broke down
in Mr. Stoddart’s case, but fortunately for him he
came across something that had power to repair
it. Better watch the weak spots. Delays are
dangerous.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jackson was very hard up—in Melbourne, too,
where credit died a violent death many moons
ago.
“I’ll call on Robertson,” he said to himself.
“ Robertson’s an old friend of mine. Besides, he’s
in a good position and cart help me if he likes.”
At ten o’clock he called on Robertson, who was
manager of the Antipodean Land Investment
Society.
Robertson listened quietly to his story and,
when it was finished, remarked, “And you really
tell me that you haven’t been able to sleep through
thinking of your troubles? Poor old Jackson 1”
Jackson replied gloomily, “ Of course I’ve not;
But, old man, I can’t and don’t expect you to
understand or even try to feel the kind of fix 1
am in—Clod forbid 1”
Robertson, with a sneer on his lips, stared
coldly at his friend for a moment, and then, smil-
ing half-eynioally, half-sympathetically, observed!
“ Poor old Jackson ! How much?”
“ I hate to say it, old man, seeing how good you
have been to me and mine already, but the fact
is ”
“Oh, say it!” exclaimed Robertson, playing
impatiently with his paper-knife.
“Well, then,” said the other, “if you can
manage it I really want ten pounds. I could do
with less, but, you see ”
“ Yes, I know—your troubles would worry you
again at the end of a week or so. Take fifty, and
postpone them.”
Robertson wrote out two cheques, e.ach for fifty
pounds, one of which he gave to Jackson, unen-
closed, and the other in an envelope addressed to
Mrs. Jackson.
“ With my best respects to Mrs. Jackson,” said
Robertson, in reference to the latter.
Jackson took the money, and with the fine,
genial glow which possesses every man who has
coin in his pocket, observed with feeling in his
voice! “Robertson, old man, you, too, seem to
have troubles.”
“Nothing to talk of,” replied Robertson,
curtly.
“But, my dear fellow, you look pale and hag-
gard,” insisted Jackson, “Aman in a good billet
like yours shouldn’t be worried in the same way
as a poor devil like me.”
“ Not at all,” answered Robertson, in a tone of
grim irony ; “ but, for God’s sake, Jackson, if you
have nothing further to say—go 1”
Jackson left, ,slightly offended by the hrusquerie
of his friend, and over a glass of whisky with
another friend, Avhorn he had asked to join him,
observed; “Damned if I can understand some
fellows. Now, there’s Robertson—you know him
—manager of the Antipodean Laud Investment
Society. He holds a good position, and yet,
when I called upon him just now about a
little business we had together, he looked, I give
you my word, 00 years old.”
“Drinks secretly, you may depend upon it,”
said the other, a man with an amorphous red nose.
Next morning Jackson, trifling over his toast
and ham and eggs—a repast suitable to a man of
his wealth-saw the following paragraph in one
of the morning papers :
Late yesterday evening the caretaker of the Southern
Cross Buildings, noticing that the door of the offices of
the Antipodean Land Investment Society was slightly
ajar, went in and found the manager, Alexander Robert-
son, sitting dead in his chair with an empty laudanum
phial in his hand. The police were called in, and an
inquest will be held to-day. The reason for the rash act
was given in a statement addressed to the directors of the
Society, left on his table by deceased. It was to the effect
that during the last two years he had fraudulently appro-
priated money to the extent of £3OOO belonging to the
Society.
Jackson read out the paragraph to his wife—a
handsome, black-haired, Herodias-faced woman.
“Poor old Robertson !” he said. “It was as I
told a friend of mine yesterday — he had his
troubles, too.”
“He had,” remarked Mrs. Jackson, white to
the lips.
And over the fire in the kitchen she burnt a few
lines of writing, which had accompanied the fifty-
pound cheque, and which read as follows ;
“ Dear Emma,— I send you the last 1 can lay my hands
on. I can stand this life no longer, . You will know all
about it in the morning.”

Colemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Pills stir one up.
A lieutenant of Vic. militia has achieved
the great end of a military existence and killed,
somebody. The wisdom of allowing this gentle-
man to defend his country can be judged from
his readiness to resist an invasion, of his back-
yard, at Moonee Ponds (Melb.) Vague stories of
a phantom intruder were circulated in the gentle-
man’s household, so after a while “ he determined
to lie in wait for the trespasser, or trespassers,
and if necessary to shoot them with his Martini-
Henry rifle.” He did lie in wait. The unknown
intruder (who seemingly haunted the premises)
was seen for more or less, of a certainty to enter
the back-yard and: clear out again. When this
mysterious figure had carefully escaped, the
lieutenant rushed into a right -of - way,
and “saw, as he thought, the robber
within twenty or thirty yards of him. Wishing
to cu, tnre him, and thinking to frighten him into
submission, he pointed the riHo at the man, who
walked towards him hurriedly as though about to
atlark him. Fearing attack, the officer fired, in-
tending to hit tin* man in the logs—and lay him
out. Also, he reloaded his gun so as to be “ pre-
pared for any emergency.” However, there was
no occasion for a second bullet. The lieutenant
had fatally wounded an auctioneer in the stomach
with his first shot. And this is oneof the warriors
called out during the “strike ” to keep the work-
ing man from doing any mischief. The Bulletin
is sorry for him, of course, but even more sorry
for the auctioneer :
Lieutenant, from the tears you wore,
We may surmise you often swore
To shed a fellow-creature's a ore
In warfare grim and lawful.
“ 0, let him like a soldier fall I ’
You probably were wont to bawl.
You meant no harm, but, clash it all!
This incident is awful.
You polished up your sword with zest:
You longed, if truth be full oonfess’t,
To stick a medal on your chest
When gallantly you’d won it.
You skirmished round the other night
In search of secret toe to fight;
You meant to lay him out all right—
And now you’ve gone and done it.
Pray, bid adieu to martial “ fun,”
Get rid of that accursed gun ;
To plans for making Russians run
Be evermore a stranger.
You’ve shot a harmless neighbour dead :
In point of fact you lost your head.
’Tis soldiers such as you we dread.
(Lord, keep us out of danger !)

Even! —A Story of Borneo.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Tire not thy tongue, Dusi-Mota ! My
daughter’s word is my word. All good be unto
thee, but when everything is said thou art but a
Kayan, and the Sea-Dyaks like not to mate with
the seratohers of the soil!”
Little Dusi’s innumerable brass rings clinked as
he struck his hands together above his head, and
his answer spluttered fiercely through his file-
pointed teeth.
“Glad enough are the Sea-Dyaks to use the
Kayan swords ! There is not a man among ye—
Saghais and Illinoans and all —that could forge
such a parang as that at thy side, O father of
Sinsha ! No better are your own blades than the
rotten steel of the white man !”
Muniad turned a fond eye upon his weapon,
bedecked with tufts of dyed human hair. “Truth
is with thee,” he acknowledged. “From the
grave of a Kayan chief came this, and never did a
better one cut pig.* But, what of that ? Cowards
may well make weapons for brave men to use !”
The Kayan’s yellow face turned green, and his
hand went to his side, but brought therefrom only
his rattan-knife, with which from the bunch of
pendants at his ear he cut away a tiger-cat’s
tooth, spat thrice upon it, flung it at Muniad’s
feet, and went.
“Do thy worst —son of a crocodile’s uncle!’’
the Sea-Dyak called after him. “ Sinsha laughs
at thee, and Sinsha’s father fears nob thee nor all
thy filth-eating tribe!’’
* * •
The white man sat on the platform of
Muniad’s house and talked of many things, while
the moon climbed out of the dusky eastern forests
and sparkled against the faces of the long rollers
marching rhythmically in upon the beach below.
The white man asked questions, the brown man
answered them, and Sinsha winnowed rice and
listened. The broad equatorial moon sent a sheen
off her glossy skin, struck fire from the brass
wire coiled upon her shapely arms, and deepened
the shadows of her long-lashed eyes. “ What a
pity that another ten years will see her a withered
old hag !” thought Medlow. But he might have
spared his commiseration on that head.
Talk turned upon the sumpitan, and Sinsha,
dropping fan and shovel, ran to fetch the instru-
ment. Medlow looked anxiously at the eight-foot
tube, with its heavy spear-blade fixed, bayonet-
like, outside the line of propulsion, and Muriad
laughed to see how respectfully the other handled
the sago-thorn darts. No danger with these
arrows, explained the Dyak. The no
poison after a few hours’ exposure to the air. And
then he brought the little bamboo tube into which i
the potent upas-sap had been trebly sealed with j
wooden plug, beeswax, and lizard-skin. A “ hand j
of the sun ’’—about fifteen minutes—a man might
live, he said, if the poison were fresh upon the
arrow that wounded him.
Medlow laughed incredulously. He was but a
book-naturalist at that time, and his knowledge of
the stryehniferous Astocarpce was theoretical.
“I’ll believe it when I see it!” he said, and for
answer Sinsha clapped a hand to her neck, sprang
to her feet, and held out to her father—a sumpitan
arrow ! “What dog has ’ the old man was
beginning furiously, when, piff! another of the
tiny darts stood in his own shoulder. Then he
guessed. “ Dusi!” he shouted.
“ Ay, Dusi!” said a voice from below, where
stood in the moonlight a squat, dark figure, with
the deadly tube still levelled at the platform.
Medlow whipped out his Dean-and-Adams, but
the Kayan went with a ten-foot spring into a
rhododendron-clump.
“ I see thee, white master ! ” he cried in Malay;
“ but thou canst not see me. Therefore shoot nop,
neither come down hither, unless thy skin is
thicker than Muniad’s —or Sinsha’s ! ” and the
little devil cackled hideously amongst the
branches. .
But Sinsha was already past hearing, and the
old man, swaying as he stood, repeated his slayer’s
advice. “ Why shoulds’t thou die also, and use-
lessly?” he said. “As for me, lam old, and my
daughter goes with me into the mists.”
“Good God ! ” cried Medlow, “ is there nothing
to be done?”
“Nothing that thou canst do. Take now thy
tirne-measurer and wait to see if ray words were
truth.” . ,
Medlow looked at the dying girl and sighed,
but then—scientist to the marrow—he took out Ins
watch. The moonlight showed the half-hour after
seven. When the hands stood at ten minutes to
eight he knew that Muniad was right!
CAPHICORNUS.
Colemane & Sons’ Yio. Ointment is marvellous.
Mashonaland Blue Book contains the full
text of the messages between Dr. Jameson, of the
Chartered Co., and Lobengula. The Matabele
King complains at length of being robbed of
cattle, and, generally, of unfair treatment. His
last dispatch reads : —
July 27th.-My own messengers have arrived, and they
tell me that the captured cattle you complain of as belong-
ing to the Company have been duly returned ; but you
did not tell me that you had a lot of the Amahoh cattle
hiding with you, together with their owners, and that,
when my indunas claimed them from Captain Lendy, he
refused to give up either cattle or men, and told my
indunas that the Amaholis and their cattle did not
belong to me any longer and then turned his cannon on
to my people. Arc the Amaholis then yours, including
their cattle ? Did you, then, send them to come and steal
mv cattle? Capt. bendy said you had bought them tor
money. Where, then, did you place the cash? Whom
did you 'dve it to ? Let my cattle be delivered to my
people peacefully. I wish you to let me know
at once. I thought you came to dig gold ; but it
seems that you have come not only to »olcl
but to rob me of my people and country as well. Remem-
ber that you are like a child playing with edged tools.
Tell Capt. Lendy he is like some of r*, own young men.
He has no holes in his ears and cannot or will not hear.
He is young, and all he thinks about is a row’. Hut \ou
had better caution him carefully or he will cause trouble,
serious trouble, between us.
* With a downward stroke of the parang many Dyaks
can cut a moderate-sized pig clean in two.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
S tilings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
°te* or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail , but is entirely
m the ha nds of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Vetse, Short dales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable ),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 25, 1893.
The Central Division.
Whenever the “ unemployed ” problem
assumes a more than usually aggravated
form in New South Wales the question of
throwing the Central Division open for
selection invariably comes to the front.
The “unemployed” question is always
present in all the Australias, both in good
seasons and in bad, though of late the seasons
have all been about equally bad so far as the
labourer is concerned. But most of the
time the difficulty is allowed to take care of
itself. It is only when some paper that is
unusually short of a sensation sends out a
representative to count the sleepers in
Sydney Domain, and to interview the home-
less vagrant in his barrel or his gin-case,
that there is a spurt of activity upon the
subject. Then the situation generally
resolves itself into something like this ; The
industries at present existing will not absorb
all, or nearly all, the workers ; the social
system has somehow got out of joint and a
million consumers no longer supply employ-
ment for a million producers—no one knows
exactly why. The Treasury is empty, and
the old device of absorbing “surplus labour”
on unprofitable public works, paid for with
borrowed money, is utterly impracticable
—also, past experience has shown it
to be futile as well as impracticable. The
ancient Soup Kitchen device is ruinous and
utterly hopeless. The only prospect is,
somehow or other, to find the famished
multitude employment on the land—the
multitude which has no capital, no tools,
and in many cases no knowledge of agri-
culture; to shunt as many as possible of them
out into the wilderness with a spade and a
hoe, and leave the rest to Providence.
Therefore, there is a periodical attack upon
the squatter who bars the country against
the agriculturist, and a demand that the
Central Division shall be “thrown open.”
* # *
There is very little semblance of logm
about this frequent war-whoop. Moreover,
in these days, the squatter is by no means so
bloated as is commonly supposed. In many
ways, no doubt, he has managed to make
himself a public evil. He has done his
utmost to boss the West after the manner of
the Virginian cotton-planter in the good old
days before M‘Clellan’s legions appeared
in the Peninsula, and the first signs of the
downfall of the Slave Confederacy began to
be apparent. He is generally a cheap-
labour man of the cheapest and most
aggressive description. He is almost
always a J.P., and his legal decisions
are only fit, as a rule, to be burnt
by the common hangman. Also, he invari-
abl} 7 does his utmost to extinguish trades-
unionism and to abolish political liberty, and
to support the subservient Chinese leper
and similar disasters. Yet, despite all this,
the squatter is not the unmitigated curse he
is commonly represented to be. He is at
least a producer, and his industry brings
almost as much money into Australia as all
others combined. If the State would only
take some radical measures to prevent him
being swallowed up by banks and British
money-lending corporations, which now
scoop in half the profits of the wool industry
and use them to pay big dividends in Lon-
don ; and some reasonable limit were put to
the size of individual leaseholds, and the
absentee wool-grower were suppressed with a
strong hand, and the pastoral J.P. utterly
wiped out, he would even be comparatively
unob j ectionable.
* # #
Moreover, the present advantages of
throwing open the Central District are more
than problematical. Much of it wants an
expensive system of irrigation to make it
anything like a reliable field for agriculture.
It is too far from the principal market, and
the selectors produce, if it is to be carried
at rates which will make farming operations
reasonably profitable, must also be carried
at rates which will make the railway system
a dead loss, which loss must be recouped out
of the general revenue. The proposal to re-
sume half of each run means that the great
plain is to be dotted over with scattered
agiicultural settlements, and the agriculturist
on the more remote of these settlements
must nay freight on his produce over miles
and miles of pastoral country where every
mile means a steady diminution of his profits.
The country is not especially fertile, so far as
is generally known. And when the State
has thrown open a great portion of the land
and thereby done considerable damage to
the wool industry, and seriously diminished
the country’s most profitable export, the
chances are that the selector will promptly
go to wreck through remoteness, drought,
want of sufficient roads, and other disadvan-
tages.
• * «
. is no fc the Central but the Eastern Divi-
sion which wants to be thrown open to the
agriculturist. The land is the only resource
of the multitudinous unemployed, but the
region on which they should be settled is the
well-watered coast-lands—the country where
rain is plentiful, and roads are good, and
railway couirnuiiication is comparatively
easy, and freights are light because a market
is close at hand. There is still enough room
in the Eastern Division for every unemployed
citizen of New South Wales who is capable
of making a livelihood by agriculture and is
willing to do so. The oft-repeated tale
about the scarcity of land is a lie and a
myth, for the unoccupied soil is there in
abundance. When all the coast region
which is suitable for agriculture is occupied,
then it will be time to begin moving the
squatter back into the West. And when
it is so occupied, because of the density of
the population and the increased railway
traffic which will follow, it will be possible
to reduce railway freights to a point which
will make agriculture profitable in regions
further back, without making the railway
system a dead loss and a burden on the
revenue.
* * *
It is not the squatter who wants to be shifted
wholesale at present to make room for the
farmer, but the land speculator and the loan
and mortgage society and all the other
associated pawnbrokers and pawnbroking in-
stitutions, local and foreign. The pastoralist
at least supplies a small revenue to the
State, and furnishes a certain amount of
employment, and supplies an article of ex-
port which brings a vast amount of money
into the country, and he can, if Parliament
is wise enough to so decree, be allowed
a purely leasehold tenure for a certain
number of years at a largely increased
rental, without further dissipating the
public estate. But the land-speculator in
the fertile coast-region supplies no State
revenue, furnishes no employment save to a
few clerks, creates no wealth, and is nothing
save a curse and an incubus. Also, in his
various capacities, he exercises more political
influence, and exercises it to worse effect,
than the whole pastoral interest of New
South Wales. If the Democratic party in
I arliament will lay it down as the first axiom
of its faith that the man or syndicate or cor-
poration or bank that holds land for the mere
object of creating booms, and thereby blocks
the settlement of the people on the soil, is a
public enemy, and is to be taxed out of
existence, then it will be nearer a solution of
the unemployed difficulty than it has ever
yet been. Its object should be to turn the
entire Eastern District into a region of small
farms, with a limit of (say) 640 acres, each
cultivated by its owner ; and to tax the land-
holder who lets his land to sweated agricul-
turists and the landholder who does not let
his land, but keeps it for speculative pur-
poses, till they will both be compelled to sell
out and move on over the rim of the earth.
* * #
The apparent enthusiasm with which a
certain section of the Sydney press has taken
up the demand for the opening of the
Central District is merely based on a desire
to accept the less of two evils. It is neces-
sary to find room somewhere in New South
Wales for an agricultural population, or else
there are possibilities ahead which are too
dreadful to contemplate. And if the Central
District squatter, or the Eastern District
speculator has to be sacrificed, it is cheaper
to sacrifice the former. Nearly every
business man in Sydney is more or less
interested in land banks or land syndicates,
and a policy which would break up the
whole land-jobbing industry is too dreadful
to be seriously considered. It is simply the
alternative between breaking one’s own idol
and breaking the other man’s idol, and the
other man’s idol is naturally the one to go.
But, compared to the city land-speculator, the
pastoralist is a man of liberal ideas, a
blessing to his country, an ornament to
society in general, and a brilliant example
of enlightenment and progress. And, com-
pared to the unoccupied coast-lands which
the speculator is holding for the next boom,
the Central District is an arid hell, where,
under present conditions, the agriculturist,
instead of being able to find work for the
unemployed and a home for himself, is only
likely to arrive at general bankruptcy.
The Electric-Lighting Swindle.
The famous Colo Yalley electric-lighting grab
has now got to its second stage in New South
Wales Parliament. The first stage was finished
a few weeks ago by the passing of the “ Aus-
tralasian Rights Purchase Bill,” a deliberate
fraud upon posterity which will keep green
the memory of many a legislator who might
otherwise have dropped into speedy oblivion.
As an example of dishonest inconsequence
and unprincipled anti - climax, that measure
stands absolutely alone. The ostensible
reasons for its existence were that gas-lighting
is inefficient, costly, out-of-date, and otherwise
objectionable, and that Sydney and the adjacent
towns are much in need of a cheap and reliable
illuminant. Therefore the measure proceeded
to give away—not to sell, but to hand over with-
out any semblance of compensation—the only
available cheap source for the production of the
only illuminant which will answer the require-
ments of the case. Which, in the utter in-
coherence of its professed logic, would be about
on a par with an Act providing that, whereas
crimes of violence require to be repressed with
a strong hand, therefore any person convicted
of robbery and assault shall receive a banouet
and a purse of sovereigns,
# # n
The Sydney Electric Lighting Bill, which
turned up last week, was the natural corollary
of the previous job. This measure professed to
giveto Sydney Corporation authority to light the
city with electricity as a municipal undertaking,
the profits of which would go to the community
at large. If it had been introduced before the
nefarious Colo Valley scheme, the possibility is
that Parliament might have been compelled to
reject the latter, as being altogether too naked
and shameless an operation to carry out in
daylight. Then, the Colo water-right leases
would probably have been conferred on the
Corporation, which would thus have owned its
own electric-power, instead of having to buy it
from a private syndicate at almost any price
the syndicate chose to demand; and the Colo
Valley marauders would have lost a nett spoil
of at least £500,000, which amount would have
been £500,000 of clear gain to the community.
But, as it is, the syndicate owns the only avail-
able source of supply, therefore the principal
object of the Electric Lighting Bill is to furnish
said syndicate with something like 400,000
reliable customers who will supply it with large
dividends and involve the outlay of very little
capital. The syndicate, in fact, gets its power
which is really 99 per cent, of its plant, sup-
plied gratis by the State ; it gets its monopoly
from the State, also gratis ; and it is furnished
with customers on a compulsory system, by Act
of Parliament.
* * *
But the octopus monopoly wants more than
this. Even with the tremendous handicap of
having to buy from the Colo syndicate at its
own price what the Colo syndicate has got for
nothing from a brazen Legislature, there still
remain to the municipality the profits on
the distribution and sale of electricity. And
private enterprise wants to control both ends
of the monopoly. Therefore, a proviso is
inserted in the Bill providing that the City
Council may lease for a long term (42 vears was
seriously proposed by one bulky capitalist) the
privilege of lighting and supplying electric
power to the New South Wales metropolis. As
the Colo Valley Co. has the only available
supply, it will naturally get this privilege.
Ihere is no serious attempt in the Bill to limit
the price to be charged, and there is an utter
absence of any guarantee that no member of
the Council shall be interested, as a shareholder
or otherwise, in the great water-power corpora-
tion. The Bill simply provides that the
Council may give away an immensely valuable
privilege on practically any terms it pleases, to
any persons it pleases, and with any restrictions
or no restrictions at all, as it sees fit—or pro-
fesses, for reasons not stated, to see fit.
* * *
This leasing proviso was negatived last
Inday, in a very thin House, by 24 votes to 19
—the majority consisting mainly of the Labour-
party. But the defeat is only temporary. The
leasing coup is the real object of the Bill, and
the lighting of Sydney is a mere secondary con-
sideiation. therefore, when the Labour-party
amended the measure—despite the most strenu-
ous efforts of the Ministers present—to read
that the work should be done by the Corpora-
tion as a public enterprise instead of a private
make, its further consideration was imme-
diately postponed. When it comes up again,
every advocate of “private enterprise ” who is
not, sick or dead, or otherwise urgently engaged,
will be on hand. And if the Democratic party
is true to its faith, it will also be present to the
very last man, if onlv * .
enabling the electors of a r) il! r ,, the Purpose *

The Australian SiAR-an organ of private
enterprise and an inefficient Balaam that
always at hand to curse on behalf of every such
glaring scheme-has already raised the old "id
anathema about the conduct of the Labour
members, in blocking an enterprise which
would have furnished work for the hungry
unemployed.” The statement is so utter a
perversion of the truth that it is hardly likely
to impose on anybody. The Labour-members
7 , tbe , l , r pass all that part
of the Bill which promised to find work for the
unemployed. They were willing and anxious
that the municipality should get its Bill through
and proceed to supply work on the instant,
ihe Bill was blocked not by them, but by the
nineteen “private enterprise” members who
professed profound anxiety to have the under-
taking conducted by the Corporation of Sydney,
and yet refused to go on unless they were per-
mitted to sell the public rights to a syndicate,
and erect another blood-sucking monopoly in
the land. The difference between the two
parties was that the Labour-members wanted
to supply work for the people and profit for the
municipality, while the Star and its proteges
obviously wanted to supply boodle for private
enterprise, and if they couldn’t get it, then the
workless masses might starve as heretofore.
The plea of the boodle-party, no doubt, will be
that the Democrats should have accepted their
private job for the sake of the employment which
that job would incidentally provide ; but if the
sixth clause had provided for murder for mere
grab, the plea would be equally logical. The
attitude of Dibbs, Manning, “ Jack ” Want,
and the rest of the 19 can be described in three
sentences. They say to the unemployed : “ You
are hungry and hopeless. If a private company
gets the privilege of bleeding the people for 21 or
42 years they will possibly furnish employment
for a few of you. Otherwise, you may go
hang.” And there, for the present, the matter
remains.

About to be Born.

Dummy Directors.
The defences in the Mercantile Bank case
varied remarkably. For one of the direc-
tors counsel urged that “If the balance-
sheet was false or inaccurate, it was not
so to his knowledge.” Another advocate
also pleaded that there was no evidence of
his client having “ authorised or adopted the
publication of the balance-sheet,” and again,
that “there was no implied authority on the
part of the manager to issue a balance-sheet in
.the name of the directors, but, if there were, it
would only be an authority to issue a true, and
not a false, balance-sheet .” Melbourne, pro-
bably, is the only important city in the wide
world where such financial doctrines could be
advanced without exciting a general hubbub.
An admission that the Mercantile balance-sheet
might have been false was rational enough. Con-
trasted with the Matthew Davies an clMillidge
statements, in which the same sheet was
■described as a pattern of honest candour, it
was a rational admission. But to argue
that a director was not supposed to know
anything about the accuracy, or otherwise, of
the bank’s report, and that another director
was legally responsible as a director for truths
only, and not for misrepresentations, was
tantamount to saying that the signatures at the
foot of a balance-sheet might as well be supplied
by the old lady who washes the board-room
floor. Public opinion supports the statement
that Messrs. Bell and Anderson knew (for
certain) very little about the state of their
bank’s health. As men and brothers they have
the sympathy due to people who allowed
themselves to get into a mess which they
have not wilfully created. As directors,
Messrs. A. and B. are not strictly en-
titled to any sympathy at all. They
may ask for it, and the world may charitably
give it to them, but all the same they are far
from being martyrs. In this Mercantile case
there were repeated references made to the
“name of an honest man.” Any director who
had become legitimately possessed of that ex-
cellent article was said to hold a talisman
against which no criminal law might prevail.
Unfortunately an honest name, attached to a
bank director who takes the truth of balance-
sheets for granted, is but a delusion and a
snare. The more honest the name the more
it is, under such circumstances, likely to be re-
sponsible for the misplacement of public
confidence. When a gentleman takes a lucra-
tive seat at a “board,” on the strength of his
honourable name, he owes at once a special
duty to his name, even though he doesn’t under-
stand that he owes it to himself likewise.
Messrs. Bell and Anderson have declared,
from the mouths of their lawyers, that they
didn’t thoroughly fulfil this duty, and if they
are 1° he neld utterly irresponsible for the Mer-
cantile Bank smash, The Bulletin would like
0 know what is the meaning of directors’ fees,
what is the official value of the name of an
honest man ? As Matthew Davies, Millidge
and Muntz have been committed for trial, these
wo pertinent questions are directed at the
other four defendants—the ones who have been
acquitted of all suspicion of dishonesty.

two weeks before it arrived.
Evidently an animal like this has come to Aus-
tralia to stay. The Asiatic camel-driver may
be got rid of, but the camel will remain. If the
ungainly beast with the hump is a cause of
alarm to horses, then so much the worse for the
horse; the camel can’t be abolished on his
account any more than can the locomotive
because it is a cause of wild surprise to a goat
drawing a hand-cart.
Three extracts from the Freetrade Camper-
down (Vic.) Chronicle :
As showing the importance of the dairying industry in
this district, which has been developed by the wisely
directed assistance of the State, it may be mentioned that
the amount paid to the producers last month exceeded
£7OO, while the pay-sheet for this month will reach about
£lOOO. The butter-factory here is proving the saviour of
the farmers.
That’s Protection in its most undisguised form.
Next :
The farmers of this district are reminded that Mr.
Hirscii will lecture here next Tuesday night on the
benefits and advantages of Freetrade. In these days,
when it has become apparent that a protective policy
spells ruin, the lecture should be brimful of interest.
That’s Freetrade. Next item is an extract
from Hirsch :
Protection is demoralising to the community generally,
but especially disastrous to the farming interest. If
returned I will oppose all duties or grants towards
starting butter factories, wineries, meat freezing or beet
sugar industries, not because these are not desirable, but
because freetrade teaches that all such should not be
interfered with by the State, but left to private enter-
prise.
Which effectually damns the first paragraph,
and knocks the bottom out of that local butter-
factory that is proving the saviour of the
farmers. It is very hard to be a Freetrade
paper, for every Freetrade paper wants Protec-
tion for its own local interest and Freetrade
for the rest of the country.
Leprosy.
Dear Bulletin, —“Medicus”’ brain must surely
have been woolgathering. There is not the
slightest record of there ever having been 500
lepers in all Australia, much less in N.S. Wales.
As a matter of fact, although the medical pro-
fession in Australia have been constantly on the
look-out for the disease, and though the country
has been ransacked for lepers, only 73 cases have
been officially recorded on this continent during
the last ten years, and 54 of these have been
Chinamen. In N.S. Wales, in the last ten years,
there have been but 43 known cases of leprosy,
of which 22 were reported during the years
1891-1892. In their report laid before Parliament
on 25th March last, N.S.W. Board of Health
says :
The Board does not regard this large increase—in '9l
and ’92—as any indication of the spread of the disease, as
many of the cases recently admitted must have been suf-
fering from the disease for some years past; but it was
only when the reporting of these cases was made com-
pulsory by law that their friends and medical attendants
notified the Board.
Moreover, neither the senior nor the junior sur-
geon at Little Bay has anything to do with the
interning of lepers under the Leprosy Act. Ih.
Act is administered by the Board of Health, who**)
Inspector, Dr. Ashburton Thompson, is the
leprosy-ex pert, and it is admitted by all who
are capable of judging that Dr. Thompson is
singularly well fitted both by knowledge and
training to diagnose leprosy at any stage of the
disease. There is no necessity whatever to send
anyone away from N.S. Wales to study leprosy;
as well send someone to study measles.
“ Medicus ” writes : “ Were an expert in leprosy
to visit all the Chinese camps he could a tale
unfold,” &e. Such a visitation took place last
year, by order of the Board of Health, of all the
N.S.W. Chinese camps, “ and no further cases of
leprosy, or diseases resembling leprosy, were
found.” There was no necessity for “ Medicus
to work up a leprosy-scare. Leprosy is a declining
disease all the world over, and to us in N.S.W.
is of far less importance than consumption. In
fact, the Indian Leprosy Commission has just
reported against the contagiousness of the disease.
[Queer that Fat her Dam ien became a leper.—Ed. B. J
In Maoriland, leprosy has almost burnt itself out.
Wi Mahi, the aged chief of the Ngatirangiwewehi,
living at Awahou, on Lake Rotorua, lately de-
clared —
There is no ngerengere now as compared to what it was
when I was a boy. I have known it carry off whole hapus.
I attribute our immunity to the fact that all the old men
who knew— wero-ngerengere— how to communicate the
disease have died out. My father was the worst of them.
He destroyed numbers by giving them ngerengere. His
plan was to make his enemy a present of a mat or some
r!ihpr article of clothing that had been worn by a ngeie
S. and the disease wassure to follow. [Anotherproof
of the contagiousness of leprosy.— Ed. B.J
The Maoris believed that the disease was intro-
duced to Hauraki by a sailor marooned from a
whaler, at the end of the 17th century, and that
it was spread by wero-ngerengere— which is the
act of communicating the disease by puncture or
inoculation. The Maoris do not believe leprosy
to be contagious nor hereditary. At the London
Hospital, where Mr. Jonathan Hutchinson always
kept* one or two cases of leprosy for educational
purposes, they were treated as ordinary patients
Fn the general wards. These facts may reassure
Sadars whom “Medicus” may have
frightemdl-Yours. 4o„ Newcastle,
N.S.W.

To His Excellency.
Tiresome is the twaddle in Melb. press re Gov. Hope-
toun’s dilettante attempts at agriculture. This nice
young man had about three and a-hail acres of richly-
manured land in the Domain put under crop to work ex-
periments for the benefit of the farming interest.
Numerous State agricultural experts and specialists and
much machinery were employed, and no expense was
snared to make the crop worthy of His Ex. Horny-
handed and impecunious farmers were invited to roll up
at the harvesting, and see what could be done with three
and a-half acres, industry, and intelligence. The hay-
seeds came and scoffed openly and venomously at the toy
Governor and his little farm. “Turn ’im loose on a
Gippsland selection, and where’d he be?’’ said one old-
timer :—
Would you be the King, the strong man, first in
council and in toil,
To the men who war with nature for possession of
the soil ?
Take an axe upon your shoulder, take a billy and
a rug,
And go forward in the forest where no man has
cut and dug,
Where the scrub-ferns grow like magic, and the
gum-trees you must fell
Have their topmost boughs in heaven, and their
tap-roots deep as hell.
Take the land the darned Department would
devote to Smith or Brown,
Two miles or more from water and a hundred
miles from town ;
Fell, and scrub, and hew, and hunger, and when
seven weeks are gone
You may have a clearing large enough to build a
hut upon.
Then you furnish it with saplings and you carpet
it with loam,
And you bring the kids and missus to their charm-
ing country home !
Rising early with the jackass, like a man of pith
and push,
With axe in hand you sally forth to face the
mighty bush.
’Tis ten thousand trees against one man—the odds
are hard enough—
But the settler must be stubborn and the settler
must be tough,
And he strikes from morn till even with his strong
arm bare and brown,
And the ranges roar a cheer back when the big
gums rattle down.
So you slave and strive and suffer, for it’s fearful
work and slow
Ere the cabbages are solid and the spuds have
room to grow.
By and bye to fruit and fowls and swine, as city
swells advise,
You resort to make a fortune; but the venture
proves unwise,
For the fruit-trees blight and wither, and the pigs
die in their pens,
And the drought destroys the ducklings, and the
dingoes eat the hens.
Years go on and still the bush-wall rings your
narrow clearing round.
But you’ve won a few good acres and a crop is on
the ground,
And you harvest single-handed and you rake the
stubble clean,
For you have no cash to fork out and no dandified
machine;
Still you’re thankful for small mercies— though
you’re often sorely pushed—
When the missus hasn’t sunstroke and the baby
isn’t bushed.
Then, at last, when worn with work and warped
with years, and very grey,
When you’re mastering the mortgage and the rail-
road runs your way ;
When your farm is looking home-like, and your
sons are grown-up men,
You may talk to brown-faced farmers —you may
try to teach them then.
And if any kid-gloved lordling starts to give you
points on grain,
And a little hot-house farming does to make your
errors plain,
ifou will rise up with a waddy, and you’ll sympa-
A n xii udu »»
thiae with Cain.
E.D.
The youthful Sydney wood-vendor has one
unfailing dodge to effect a sale. Knocking at th e
kitchen door, lie pulls a long face and asks the
housewife to buy, “just a couple o’ bobs’ worth,”
as “ the tire o’ me wheel has come off an’ I can’t
get ome again ter-night.” And ashe has carefully
split all his billets in halves he generously offers
her 40 pieces for the “two bob.’’ The average
woman can’t resist buying anything cheap-even
billet-wood—so he generally effects a sale. Then
he goes next door with the same story.
Dodds, the Active Service Brigade leader,
who asserts that only by making themselves a
nuisance and a menace to the community can the
unemployed gain attention to their wants, is a
disciple of the S.M. Herald, and voices the
literal sentiments of that journal. The Herald’s
principle in dealing with the unemployed has
been and is, that their want of work is their ovn
concern, but that if a time comes when “ the
comfort-possessing inhabitants are rendered less
secure by reason of the presence of distressed
people.” then it is the duty of Govt, to do some-
thing. Dodds says the time has come, and exhorts
his fellows to prove it.
Parson Paton, on the other end of the
Kanaka trade:— ......
Yerv many natives who go to Queensland from this
nlace (New Hebrides) die. lam often awake at night,
hearing the death-wail of natives for Kanakas who have
died abroad. The Almighty, I believe, hears those walls
also Some come back dying. One, returned by the last
shin is in the final stages of consumption. We have many
strong hoys up to twelve years of age ; then comes a ter-
rible gap ‘‘Died abroad” is the almost invariable
answer. How is it strong men leave here, to diem Queens-
land before six months or a year ?

“Be Traiid.” What does it mean?
Messrs. Tunes and Co., the proprietors of the Boar’s
Hoad brand of Wines, inform ns that it is old
Saxon, and means “be true.” As such, it is very
appropriate to their fine Australian Wines, which
are sold at all grocers’, from Is. per bottle up-
wards. Their invalid Port, at 3s. 01., is a won-
derful wine, full of health giving properties.*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
November 22, 1893.
My Dear Moorabinda—
In “Cavalleria Rusticana,” which attracted an
over-crowded d.c. to Sydney Lyceum last Satur-
day, it is the full-toned ensemble that feeds the
e y e _ no t individual glories. Del Torre who
possesses lots of what Bernhardt called “du
charme,” and is, in plain English, altogether
scrumptious—sported a red petticoat principally,
but if she simply wore a flour-bag she would cut
an elegant figure just the same. I do hope the
Del Torre walk catches on in Sydney, for it is the
most graceful glide I have ever admired. Del
Tone walks from her hips, but without throwing
her epigastrium into undue prominence, as most
women do who walk similarly, and her carriage
should be prayerfully copied by all the flounder-
ing, striding, waddling women who strive to look
effective on foot. The only tiling I couldn’t like
about the fascinating prima-donna was the way
her hair was trained beside her ears—for all the
world like a pair of whiskers!
Signor Garuson, in grey, red and green, rather
suggested a parrokeet to the Australian eye, and
Giraud’s mulberry-velvet suit, with mauve points,
reflected great credit on his dressmaker. The
background of Sicilian peasantry was very realis-
tic-the men with their legs bandaged “like the
gout ” (which is the way the rustic Roman invari-
ably wears ’em, I believe), and the women with
broidered aprons—the colour-scheme being care-
fully worked out. The only thing that might still
farther improve the mise-en-action would be to
expose the whole chorus to the weather for forty
days to take some of the pristine newness off their
clothes. It is very possible that an Italian
peasant occasionally gets a new suit, but it is
highly improbable that the whole village would
burst forth into spick-and-span raiment at the
same period like so many butterfly grubs. By the
way, t thought the efforts of some of the liberally-
frescoed chorus to look devout in the effective
“ hymn ” scene were very interesting. Some of
the girls employed the same upward ogle to the
operatically invoked Deity as they bring to bear
on the stage boxes.
All the scintillating splendour of the opera
season is left in the able hands of the Blue Ballet,
which is a faithfully cerulean spectacle relieved by
the quartette of pink legs belonging to the leading
ladies. The dresses rather reminded me of “one
hundred recipes for cooking a turkey,” for you
have blue in every possible shade and every known
mingling of its various t men. Bartho, the fas-
cinating premiere danseuse, wore white tulle petals
with a pale-blue satin calyx (Bartho is too flower-
like in effect to have a mere skirt and jacket)
trimmed with silver fringe. D’Argo, the first
ballerina, sported pale-blue tulle and silver with
the usual shore, jutting skirtlet which looks ridi-
culously like an emu from the back view. But what
a lot of the true inwardness of the ballet-dancing
would depart if the danseuses came out without
those saucy little skirts—inadequate as they may
seem ! A coryphee performing cat’s-cradle with
her toes in plain tights wouldn’t draw half such
good stalls as she does when six inches of springy
gossamer seem to infer that all revelations are
strictly accidental.
The prettiest dresses on the crowded ballet-stage
were the pale-blue Amazons with petticoats of
lustrous silver fringe—the most suggestive were
the ribbon gowns. They had close-fitting bodices
and tights of flesh-colour with a band of dark-blue
round the hips—the shaded blue ribbon streamers
falling over all—you may guess the cool effect
presented by that branch of the Blue Ballet when
it began to kick ! I think the most artistic tights
were the dark blue, shading up to pale-grey and
powdered with silver stars, although they did
remind you of the star-light crackers of the 24th
May. I was just going to say that the Ballet
presented a wide field to the she-maaquerader,
only I mnsn’t forget that they boycott the fancy-
ball girl in Australia if she shows much higher
than the tabs of her satin boots.
Sydney Women’s Literary Society has mutely
confessed to its own excessive flatness by admit-
ting men as members—from which it is evident
that women en bloc make a signal failure of trying
to improve or amuse one another. But I don’t
suppose the W.L.S. (by the way, what will they
re-christen it?—the Mixed Literary Society?) will
set Port Jackson on fire even now, for very few
men (underlined) take kindly to tabby-governed
institutions. The youth who is eager to jump
into a clean collar every seventh Tuesday in the
month to go and read a piece of his own about
“ Blue Metal in the Middle Ages,” or something
equally spicy, to a circle of uninterested women
who behave as if the whole affair was a kind of
night-school—isn’t the sort of person to galvanise
anything larger than a tea-party, you may take
my word for it.
And even the yontn aforesaid doesn’t back up
ladies’ cbteries as much as he might, for he prefers
to air his sweetness at he-debating societies, as a
general tiling. Take Melbourne Austral Salon as
an example, where the Salonists (inter alia) have
songs and recitations by good amateur talent, amid
an environment of faint lilies in majolica bowls,
strong tea, and a member-roll of people not chosen
exclusively for their early piety and sound views
on church and State. And even with all these
light and airy attractions I’m afraid that if the
members gave a strictly Salon ball a great many
women would be compelled to dance together.
Vice-regal secretary Gallon inaugurated the
new regime (en parenthese I hear they call the
masculine invaders “ brothers ”) at Sydney
Women’s Literary, &c., by reading what a
humourist! —a long, pebbly paper that he once
read in England to broaden the minds of “ the
pooah ’’ of Somewhere or Other. By the way,
whenever Mr. Gallon arises in public—and he
shows symptoms of wanting to do it very often—
it is always carefully chronicled that he is
Governor Duff’s private writing-man, but nobody
whoever heard Mr. G. wading through a little
thing of his own would ever mix him up with
anybody else—he gives so may solid statistics to
the square foot. What a fortunate thing for
Sydney that Lady Jersey and he didn’t flourish at
Govt. House together!
The latest vice regal idea is to have the young
sprigs of the house of Duff taught to swim, and a
special merman has been laid on for the purpose.
The said merman (the keeper of Bronte baths) is
about the most eligible person to teach young
ladies the art which cometh naturally to the frog
that fancy could paint—for one thing, he is so
discreet in his-costmne. One day he was giving
a lesson to a number of girls and somebody called
to see him. But when a stout lad.y in two skirts,
a Jersey jacket, and a mushroom hat, rose like a
modern middle-aged Venus from the foam, the
visitor said, hurriedly, “It was Mr. Blank I
wished to see.” But that’s just who it was.
Is society becoming callous to the attractions of
the stage, or what is it? Now you see a tenor
being coddled in the dress-circle by a stylish
matron who carefully folds his overcoat over the
back of his chair to make things velvety for him.
The times of drawn box-curtains and limelight
Adonises slipping in unbeknownst has evidently
given way to cold flirtation in the open. I haven’t
yet decided which I like the look of best. But,
anyhow, I don’t fancy the idea of a mummer
coining into the circle with,a flower in his coat
and traces of grease-paint round his eyes (some-
times they forget to wash off all the improve-
ments) and sitting there applauding the rest of
the business. It gives the show too much the air
of a dress-rehearsal.
The arrest for debt, down South, of a certain
fascinating he-actor points the moral that infatu-
ated females don’t pay a div. How different the
situation becomes when you stand it on its head !
You never hear of Tottie Redlegs getting a bright
blue warrant at the eleventh hour —she leaves all
unpleasantness of that sort to the youth who en-
courages her. But when a man is the idol in front,
behold a bailiff’s shadow falls over the comedy.
An actor once confided to me his idea of the stage-
box girl; “ She asks for your photo—for your nice
fresh boutonniere , if she meets you on the Block.
By-and-by you are bailed up for an order, so she
may worship you on the cheap.” No wonder the
whole affair ends in “Lurcher.”
Quite a “Merry Duchess” sort of story is
running the blockade of Melb. tea-tables anent
a lively local girl. She and a male relative rode
to a rather third-class sort of race-fixture, and
while there the male relative ate (or possibly
drank) something that disagreed with him, and
began to stagger in his stride. This was too much
for some bell - bottomed - trousered gentlemen
present, who began to rain a teasing shower of
pebbles on the poor invalid just for the pleasure
of seeing him try to turn sharply round. But the
sportsmen reckoned without the lady. She bounded
off her gee-gee, dragged her male relative under a
shady tree, and then lashed out right and left with
her hunting crop d la Violet Varley flicking
Tapley’s legs in “La Mascotto ’’—only the lady
in the story left weals behind her. Also, her
language was just off the hob, so to say, which is
probably the reason why the “push” began by
resenting her interference, and finished with three
blankj’-cheers for the “lidy” whose adjectives alone
compelled their critical approval. Quite a Ouida-
esqtie story.
I hear that society in, say, Francofonte is split
in twain over the case of a fair society girl who
filled high the flowing bowl rather too often at a
swell hop, with most damaging results to the pro-
prieties. Hers was no common or conservatory
case of mild staggers, but the sort of alcoholic
“ accident” that is taken to the station-house on
a wheelbarrow if it is dressed in old boots and an
old umbrella—and society wants to know if
such things are to be tolerated ? Half the very
nicest people say she must be boycotted, and the
other half say she mustn’t, and both factions
simply revel in che pro. and con. at tea-parties,
but don’t get a step forrarder. However, as
Govt. House ignores the whole squabble, it is
probable the pros, will have it.
But, for rny part, I don’t think Society is jus-
tified in giving the cold shoulder to a fellow-sister
because she once makes a little idiot of herself in
a supper-room. Such things are often the result
of inexperience—an idea that you can quench your
thirst in champagne with the same impunity as if
it were soda —and no woman should be boycotted
unless she proves herself to be a tippleress from
malice prepense. Ido think a little solemn
palaver surrounded with the mystery so dear to
women should be gob up—a council of matrons,
with the culprit arraigned in front of them,
sounding notes of warning, coupled with gloomy
predictions of banishment to second-rate suburban
hops for the rest of her society life if she doesn’t
mend her habits—but not a more or less public
squabble as to whether Miss Dash shall be recog-
nised or not, because she drinks, don’t you know.
And to boycott her might be to make her drown
her sorrows still deeper.
Don’t you think it’s rather amusing that a
charming girl who wore subdued crape for an
aristocrat who died in Australia, and told her
friends that her engagement to him was all but
announced, should be now en route for Bull land
to wed a blonde member of the very same suite?
The person who adorns his page with furrin
quotations and can t call a necessity anything but
a sine qud non, would generally do better in plain
English, but then there are times when a little
judicious Latin acts like a charm. For instance,
I was rather amused at the prospectus of a Vic-
torian drawing mistress who announces that she
“ has made arrangements to visit Blankeville
(Please God) every Wednesday.” Is this the
knell of the neater-looking, less-obtrusively-pious
“D. V.”?
On dit that a promising, more-or-less-official
scandal, happening just now in Titipu, hasn't
divorce for its object—the lady merely tracking
hubby down to be Rep., as Mary Ann puts it,
although its botanical name is a suit for alimony.
It is only the very stupid maiden who indignantly
casts a frivolous husband adrift while he is still
paying 20s. in the £.
This is certain to he one of Lord Jersey’s anti-
podean stories. On the opening of a pump, or a
horse-trough, or something at an inland N.S. W.
township, the excited populace broke out into a
“ball ” at night, at which their Ex.’s turned up
to do the visible-idol trick, and pose on the usual
dais while the countryside flocked to gaze open-
mouthed on the glorious spectacle. But the only
drawback was that there wasn’t any dais—not even
a thinly-disguised packing-case to frame the Goy.
and party in. As the vice-regal aide preceded his
people to find out the best way to get at the usual
12in. elevation that makes all the difference, a
horny-handed committee-man brought him up all
standing with—“ Young man, there aint agoin’
to be no daze. This yer’s a democratic darnce, and
the Guvner’s got to sit on the form like the rest
o’ era.” Which his Ex. and Lady Jersey did !
It isn’t on record whether the horny-handed
patriot charged “George” two-and-six admission,
but let the lydy in free.
The reproach of the private hansom is gradually
dying from the streets of Melbourne, and a lot of
people take cabs now on the principle of whatever
comes first. Which is as it should be, for the
saints shouldn’t forfeit all the rose-leaves of life
to the sinners. The Melbourne derni-mondaine of
yore set the scorching hand of patronage on the
hansom cab so as to have those smart and con-
venient vehicles all to herself, and the trick suc-
ceeded on the principle that the naughty people
always get all the nice things. Yehicularly, Mel-
bourne society is becoming much more untram-
melled. At the Caulfield Cup, lam told, two
ladies in a gig or a buggy were quite a common
occurrence.
One of the amusing things of life is to read the
detailed bill-of-sale of the household effects of the
man whose audited arithmetic makes two and two
come to one-and-six. It is absorbingly interest-
ing to read the auctioneer’s reverent desor ptions
of the rare old Majolica and the priceless Sevres,
combined with the six-hundred-guinea piano and
the gold-mounted crumb-brush. The first worldly
commandment, “Thou shalt feather thy nest,”
seems to be taken in a very literal sense by most
of the people who are forced to sell all they have
and give to the Law.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Alhambra Variety Show,
The piping reception of “ Cavalleria
Rusticana” (Rustic Chivalry), the second pro-
duction of the Williamson - Musgrove Italian
Opera Co., at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday,
plainly showed that essence of opera (with a
bright blue unabridged ballet in tow) has
fascinated Sydney. Mascagni’s expressive and
beautiful music excited tremendous enthusiasm
from a closely-packed house, and though many
recalls were demanded only a few were acceded
to—the “Easter Hymn,” the somewhat oomic-
operatish and flimsy “Drinking Song,” and the
remarkable “Intermezzo” (illustrative of the
broken-hearted feelings of the heroine) played by
the orchestra in one of the many cessations of the
movement. It is good that the Sicilian scenery is
so artistic, because there are several gaps for you
wherein to admire the craft of Geo. Gordon.
# # #
“Cavalleria” is an operatic whirlwind.
At one moment the scene is full of screaming
peasants backing up the excited soprano, and en-
couraged by a full orchestra sawing and booming
for dear life, and the next moment they have all
swept off the stage leaving it a blank, with the
music twittering mezzo-voce for quite a few
seconds. Still, the emotion of “Cavalleria”
never lets up for an instant—you find your breath
in the empty spaces.
* # •
Honors mostly rested with the charm-
ing and dramatic Del Torre, who was the
deceived heroine, Santuzza. Her vocal method
is according to the Italian school which
teaches the nervous-sounding tremolo; but,
fortunately, Del Torre hasn’t got the irritat-
ing vibration very badly, and her attack—
especially in the upper register—is strong and
pure. But even if her vocalisation resembles the
wail of the bagpipes, her Bernhardt-ish acting
would reconcile you to the situation. In “ Caval-
leria,” a stick warbling like a lark would have a
poor show against a histrionically-gifted fog-horn,
Giraud, as the faithless Tirriddu, was the Pag-
liacoi in velvet breeches. Giraud has a good,
strong, ton-up-Druitt-street tenor, expressing
everything but tenderness (he sang the serenade
as if he was inviting the lady to come down and
be strangled), and his histrionic excitement is
marvellous to behold. However, when you reflect
that the average Italian behaves in just the same
manner if the maccaroni happens to be under-
done, it is mere provincialism to say Giraud over-
acts when tie folds his arms and strides to the
footlights like Lauri in the “ Mountebanks,” or
when lie screws his face up like “You Dirty
Boy ” for no ostensible reason. Giraud’sgrimaces
sometimes give point to the situation, and some-
times they don’t—there is anti-climax even in
frowning.
* * *
As the festive Lola, Olga Mettler made
her bow to Sydney which dhtinctly approved of
her. Memories of the hump of Tonio haunted
the gait of Signor Garuson as Alfio, and Lucia
(Signora Carmeni) allowed the hero and heroine
to weep on her Roman cravat in a very maternal
manner. At the conclusion of the opera, the
clapping of the approving house added Entre-
preneur Musgrove (hand in hand with del Torre)
and Signor Guerrera (conductor), to the line of
undulating artists strung out across the stage, and
then the blue plush curtain fell and rose again on
the still bluer ballet.
# # *
Between the two ladies who marshal the
corps de ballet there is a great gulf fixed. Bartho
dances with her eyes, her neck, her finger-tips;
D’Argo leaves the brunt of the business to her
brawny legs. On the whole, Bartho’s method is
the more alluring. A danseuse may be doing
wonders on her toes, but if she keeps her trunk as
stiff as a plank, and jerks her head as if the figure
moved by wires, a man is apt to say “ wonderful!”
and consult his programme as to what’s next. It
isn’t the intricate goosestep alone that crowds the
front stalls. A girl who dances with a stiff neck
might just as well toe-and-heel it, clutching on to
a balancing-pole. Spectacularly considered, the
blue ballet just about tops the Lyceum record for
cold and glittering splendour.
** * *
“Fatinitza” was nob succeeded by “GiroflA
Girofla”at Sydney Opera House as announced
the show never got so far as that. The season, in
fact, began and ended with “Fatinitza.” For a
few nights there were faint indications of pros-
perity, and then the speculation fell with our old
friend, the dull, sickening thud, and the Opera
House curse is left in possession of the premises
as usual. The curse is too often the last thing to
quit the premises, and it has once more proved
itself a reliable and efficient malediction. “Fati-
nitza ” was a really good show, and Miss Cecily
Staunton alone should have been sufficient to keep
it going. Yet, if rumour is to be relied upon, the
last week’s profits were hardly sufficient to allow
of the principals getting their hair cut, unless they
had private means to fall back upon. The dis-
embodied malediction is the only thing that
has prospered of late at the Opera House.
# # »
Even the man who gets away from it alive
seems to take the curse with him. Majeroni died
—some people say—through it. His partner,
Wilson, had no luck after he left it. Minnie
Palmer left her peace of mind there, and went
away to be frenziedly pursued by her infuriated
husband, “Yours merrily, John Rogers.” The
same curse led Rogers to make a fool of himself in
the correspondence columns of the papers, and,
later on, to bang his wife and start divorce pro-
ceedings. Sheridan had the last of a long series
of fits there and died on the premises. Some
of the remains of another co. which tried to
resuscitate the old house are still supposed to be
sleeping in the Domain. The Opera House also
practically finished good old B. N. Jones; and
many other people who haven’t yet died of it can
look back on their season there as their last flicker
of good luck. The Bulletin advises the next co.
to turn round three times before starting business.
¥* * *
“ Man to Man,” a drama with a soul-
lifting railway accident in it, and much elevating
red fire, and a moral and educational free fight, is
promised next Saturday at Sydney Majesty’s.
The free fight is particularly recommended as a
bright and instructive unpleasantness and a
thoroughly moral shindy, calculated to raise the
tone of the community,
** * *
At time of going to press, the “Colleen
Bawn” was announced for Wednesday at Sydney
Royal, with Miss Maggie Moore as the Colleen,
and the priest and the informer by other members
of the co. There is always a tolerably steady
demand for Irish drama in Sydney, so the venture
ought to pay. The reason of this demand is
doubtless the instinctive sympathy which exists
between the local aristocracy and the inevitable
old Irish family in the drama, whose hereditary
domain of Ballyhooley has passed into the, hands
of the attorney, leaving them nothing but their
pride and their pedigree—which latter dates back
to the days of King Brian O’Rorke, who was
killed with a stone axe by King Fergus O’More
in the year 46.
** * *
“The Guardsman” was Saturday’s pro-
duction at Melbourne Princess’—for three nights
only. Brough and Bouoicault might well have
omitted the warrior from their list, for he didn’t
seem to come quite up to the standard. There
are many doors and a painful quantity of familiar
incident in this comedy. Women, rushing one at
a time into embarrassing situations, are stowed
away in dark bedrooms, for the purpose of scream-
ing when the harmless, necessary uncle wants to
enter and disrobe himself. The frenzied behaviour
of the central young bachelor is created by anony-
mous letters, and accounted for by the old sun-
stroke theory. Altogether, “ The Guardsman ”is
a much mofe ancient modern comedy than one
looks for nowadays. Its best joke, on Saturday,
was an unrehearsed effect. Cecil Ward, fall-
ing helplessly into the arms of a long and
slender y'oung man, brought the pair of them
to grief, the collapse of the long young man’s
legs being a brilliant success. Soon after this an
explosion of the whole narrative took place, where-
upon the crusty uncle brought proceedings to a
close by shuffling off to get a warm bath. Haddon
Chambers’ weak sentimental sketch, “ The Open
Gate,” acted as a lever de rideau. After having
left the gate open for many years, Mrs. Brough
was rewarded by the return of’her long-lost love—
a pretty, but purely stagey, idea. In real life, a
cow maunders upon the premises and eats the
fuchsias, if you leave your gate open. Pinero’s
“Lady Bountiful” was announced for Wednesday
and Thursday of this week. To-morrow (Friday)
they have a grand military and naval performance

The Royal is closed again, Gourlay and
Walton having retreated in good order upon the
modest profits of race-week. The Alhambra,
where Shine and Frank Clark seem to have great
influence over the masses, is doing pretty well,
and a promise of three remarkable acrobats was
sufficient to crowd the house on Saturday, The
work of the strangers was neither original nor
neat, but they perspired freely.
** * *
“Captain Fritz” flourishesto a small extent
at the Bijou. It is a simple-minded play with one
lively song in it. Also, the spectacle of a child in
a long night-gown, blowing soap-bubbles at the
kindly Arnold, affords a good deal of pleasure to
the ladies. “Captain Fritz” has a tragic side,
too. An appalling gipsy, acting on behalf of a
colonel with a high-bred scowl, skulks along the
play in quest of certain “papers.” Having
secured the papers he goes on to purloin the right-
ful heir, but Arnold tracks this wild outlaw to his
den, and a detachment of soldiers manages to
capture the papers before they oan do much
damage. The exact fate of these important docu-
ments is not made clear. They are probably put
back into their hiding place near the mantel-piece.

** * *
Melbourne’s bank-clerks and other aristo- 1
cracy had a haughty time at the Alexandra last
Friday and Saturday nights. On these two
special occasions the members of the Lyric Club
kept away from Government House for the pur-
pose of performing “Cloches de Corneville.”
Apart from their noble birth, the Lyrics (who
have professionals to conduct, stage-manage, and
paint the scenery) are very little better than
another, and bona fide, amateur company in the
same opera. In some parts they are not nearly as
good, but this remark applies only to the stage
portion of the show. As a display of fresh, young
Johnnies before the footlights “Les Cloches” was
a brilliant success. The scenes between the acts
when the gentlemen strolled outside to form
themselves into distinguished groups, will not
easily be forgotten. On Saturday, they give one
more performance of the operaw for the benefit of
Roland Cunningham, whose tenor voice is to
undergo a course of European training.

Melbourne people have retained their
b Kim-time ideas about theatrical shows, and will
go to only the one place that offers a first-class
entertainment, doncherknow. They might encour-
age some of the other houses to launch out it
they had the money to spare for seats. They
haven’t. The Princess’ is consequently doing all
the trade, and the opposition shows are practically
no opposition worth mentioning. The city cannot
keep more than one expensive management going,
nor will it enthusiastically patronise the cheaper
article. The Princess’, which used to get out of
fashion very easily in the days of strong competi-
tion, has settled down now to a steady average of
success, and W. and M. say that the present
year is actually the best they have ever had at the
theatre.

oF deluded cripples part, with their guineas to the greasy
one under the impression that he is somehow or other

Another benefit is to be tendered the veteran who, we

The art of stage dying—particularly of death by gun-
shot—is still in a very primitive state. The stage villain,
when he is shot through the heart, simple falls back-
wards in a tremulous heap, and the rest of the co. stand
round and look at his remains. But the man who is
really shot through the heart generally leaps several feet
in the air, whirls both arms out like two semaphores,
turns half round, and drops. Sometimes he does a short,
1 .111 desperate, gallop straight ahead, collides hard against
some obstacle, and then goes backwards. At other times
he lu. ns a somersault, or even two somersaults, hut he
always does the preliminary jump. It is practically a
certainty, and otherwise he may do anything in the way
of frantic exertion, except fly. As the audience, how-
ever, would probably regard this kind of realism as bur-
lesque, it might he as well, in future, to give up shooting
the villain through the heart and aim at his head in
preference. .
