Christchurch (M.L.) Truth: “Numbers of self-styled
medical electricians, encouraged by the example of quack
Richard, are now treating the credulous up and down the
country. Young man, disinclined to dig and afraid to
beg, buys medical coil with two handles. Substitutes
sheet of zinc,for handles and covers them witli damp
mats. Patient stands on mat and young man rubs him.”

and his friend don’t speak.
At Adelaide recently a young bushman went raving

Cyolorama. He was found naked on a bridge, and when

A number of black female heathen, known as “ the
Amazons,” are appearing at the London ’alls. They have
a queen of their own, Queen Gumma, who, being asked
by an irreverent bookmaker if she had ever been in love,
said “ Wynut?” This simple womanly question was an
extinguisher. Wynut, indeed ? It is only in white social
circles that healthy women are expected to regard love as /
a somewhat unnatural offence. /

Fillis's Circus, after a lean lime in Victorian rural dis-
tricts, has migrated to Tasmania. The Fitzgeralds of late
were generally a hit ahead of the rival firm, hut in Tas-
mania the energetic Fillis lakes first innings, so the Filzs.
will probably steam off to Maoriland meanwhile. For a
big circus there is no rest, except in disbandment. You
can’t play it with a small economical cast, nor can you
put a lot of horses and carnivorous animals on haif-tucker
or throw them out upon iheir own resources for a few
weeks. If the show is to be kept together it must always
be chasing the main chance.

the billy in the front yard. The horny-handed said he
had come at five o’clock, for he was bound to get a front
seat, even if the potatoes weren’t planted till after Xmas.
His neighbours had told him the houses were so full, that
it cost late comers four shillings to look in through the
window.

Ibsen has perpetrated another drama. Principal char-
acters —a long, grisly, leaden Norwegian parson and Ins
wife. They live at the bottom of a sunless valley, and
the parson talks lo his wife and child, chiefly about hell
and the horrors of endless torment. Child falls ill owing
to the dampness and gloom of their home, but the old,
hard, dank preacher won’t allow it to he removed ; he
in a deep, hollow voice that “God demands complete
sacrifice.” Child dies, and the mother cherishes its clothes
as the one comfort left her. But one night a gipsy and
her infant come along, and the preacher makes his wife
hand over all the treasured garments down to the last
rag. After they are gone he asks her sternly if she has
made the sacrifice of her own free will, and when she
answers in the negative, he replies that it has been made
in vain, and that she is lost. The drama closes with a
distracted woman tearing her hair and shrieking insanely
that she has seen Jehovah and is going to die, while the
preacher glowers at her out of his sunken eyes in the
background and spi>iiually—casts her off as one who is
given over lo the powers of evil.

The Wild-Cat Column.
Premier Dilibs asserts that his bill to make
bank-notes legal tender in ail parts of N.S. Wales outside
Sydney is constructed, not in the interests of the banks
but entirely in those of the public. It is gratifying, of
course, to know this, hut it seems a pity that he wasn’t
a little more explicit. The public is to be compelled to
take paper money that may he absolutely unsecured, for
a hank can issue up to £666,666 on the strength of its
capital alone, wilhout any cash reserve whatever, and the
said capital may be all out of the province, or it may have
been all lost like the capital of the Mercantile Bank, or
all lent to some insob ent teetotallers, like that of the
Federal. Anil if this sort of thing is good for the public,
then the public would he glad to know where the goodness
cornea in.
« • *
There is no doubt that the old banking system
was shiftless and precarious to the last degree. A hank
might have 100 branches scattered all over the province,
li k pi perhaps £5OOO in gold at each of the inor" impor-
tant branches, as an inducement to somebody lo break in
and kill the manager. It had, perhaps, £200,000 worth
of notes in circulation, and these were all payable at any
or every office of the hank throughout the country ;
therefore, every' branch was alwavs living on the verge of
suqi -nsion and trusting to the chapter of accidents. The
device of making notes payable at the head orti- e only is
a guarantee that, if the bank lias any gold, it will he con-
centrated just where it will he wanted. Also, it is a
guarantee that when the insolvency comes, the man in
Sydney will have a reasonable chance of getting ids
nioney, and there will also he a decent show for the man
at Parramatta ; and the prospects of the man at Dubho
won’t amount to much, and those of the person at Broken
Hill or Wilcannia won’t he worth a moderate-sized i-uise.
The man furthest back, in fact, is to be compelled lo
take his chance by Act of Parliament, and simply because
of that the time will come when the hank-note furlh st
hack will only be worth 2s. 6d. in the open market. It is
for this reason that no paper which isn't support'd against
panics by a State guarantee should be made legal lender
anywhere.

The Dibbs Government proposes to increase the
note tax from 2 to 24 per cent, as ihe price of this con-
cession. The banks are to pay this tax, and this fact
alone is a proof that the bill is for the benefit of the hanks
and not for that of the public. The pretence of granting
a concession to the public and making the hanks pay for
it is too thin to pass current outside a lunatic asylum.

The Bank of Australasia declares a div. at the
rate of per cent, per annum, and carries forward
£10,728 to next half-year. The diminution in the profits
and the consequent reduction in the div., arise prin-
cipally through the necessity for keeping an unusually
large reserve. The hank has over £3,000,000 in cash, over
£2,000.000 in loans repayable at call or on short notice,
and £899,000 in easiiy-realisalile Government securities,
making about £6,000,000 readily available in case of
emergency.
The Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment
and Guarantee Corporation, of Sydney,- has finally
“ arranged ” some £29,000 worth of deposits, to he paid
by instalments at 12, 18, 24, 30, and 36 months. The total
liabilities, actual and contingent, apart from deposits, are
put down at £163,000, not £287,000 as formerly reported,
the mistake having arisen-through the confused arith-
metic of some clerk not definitely stated, and who is still
at large.
# * *
The Mutual Provident Land, Investing, and
Building Society of Sydney (in liquidation, as a matter of
course) publishes an account of the second year of its
insolvency. The account shows that during the year
£2873 has been called up from shareholders, £l5OO has
been borrowed to keep the insolvency from going broke,
and £1924 has been received from borrowers and as rents
and instalments on land-purchases. On the other side of
the account, £4399 has been paid in reduction of liabilities,
and the rest -some £2300-has gone in interest, repairs,
taxes, and costs of liquidation. The situation is, there-
fore, that the co. has raised £4373 by calls and floating
loans, and has paid off £4399—in other words, the whole
legitimate revenue of the co. just about pays liquidation
and other expenses, and all the money wanted to wipe ofl
liabilities has to be provided by the shnrelioldeis. Under
such conditions as these, it looks as it it would be about
as cheap to shut up the premises and let the assets slide.

David Gaunson’s address for the Mercantile
Bank defence was a gem of specious irrelevancy. Saying
little about the balance-sheet, and ignoring the Daviesian
tentacles aspect of the case altogei her, Guunson wandered
outside the evidence to find special cause why the
Bench must not commit anybody for trial. Bryan
O'Loghlen was rammed down the magisterial throat as a
“ European authority ” whose nolle prosequi admitted of
no further doubts. By way of proof that Sir Mai tliew's two
dozen little banks were honestly treated as solvent in
the books of the main supply bank, it was impressed upon
the Bench that Sir Bryan’s father had been an Irish
Master of the Rolls, and' Sir Bryan's brother had held a
legal billet in a Gladstone Ministry'. All of which went
for about as much as the fact that Matthew Davies
had paid 200 guineas for his Speaker’s robes.

Gann son ; “Simpson bore a charmed life. If
Justice had her rights, where would Simpson be? There
was only one answ r." Quite right. So far as we can see

State-Auditor-General of financial companies suspected to

• * *
The Mercantile Bank proceedings, according
to Oaunson, were “ initiated by a speculative prosecution
which trusted to luck and hoped to send in the bill to
Govt, hereafter.” It would be a very verdant financial
prosecution that spent its money on the chance of getting
recouped by the Palterson Government. But any sort
of bluff was endured in this extraordinary case. The
papers printed the above fiction whilst omitting
portions of Gannson's harangue which might open the
eyes of the dullest reader. For instance, it somehow
didn’t suit the "great” Journals to publish Gannson’s
threatening remark to the Bench -“ A committal of these
men would create the greatest consternation among the
banking community.” Yet that was the most significant
statement made during the whole course of the Davies
scandal.

The end of the world must be at hand this time.
People on ’Change are saying that the Bank of England
has poured untold gold into “crook" companies, a

directors of the great British fetish to jail. And when
the Ba ik of Hingland forfeits its hoary name for honour
the Heavens will open, Michael will play his last trump

The Vic. railway employes are beginning to
agitate for a co-operative supply store. Good. If they
form themselves into one large company, procure a
shrewd, honest martinet of a manager, start the business
on a strictly cash system, and buy all their goods at tie ir
own shop, they will presently find that £1 is worth
255. And if they yo the way of moat other Australian
store shareholders, appointing a half-foolish, half-roguish
committee, and demanding “just a little” credit to
begin with, and insisting I hat half the blessed store shall
lie sent home to them “on approval,” then the Vic.
railway employes can reckon on making a melancholy
mess of it. Thus far the spirit of co-operation has never
shown itself in Melbourne, anyway. The stores there
have been p'aces where incapable friends or r> lai ives of
the committee found Billets, and where the average
customer wants a p nnd of butter deliver* d at some far-
off spot by special cart. A'so, the wife of the commit ec-
ru hi has usually found that the competent en p!oy£t
“ b haved rudely ” to her, and they were discharged.
In fact, the great idea of co-operation was to hold an
interest, large or small, in a place that offered special
opportunities for making yourself infernally unpleasant.

A N.S.W. official assignee carried an appeal in
an insolvent estate, lately, from the Acting-Registrar to
the Judge in Bankruptcy, and from the Judge to the
Full Court, and having been worsted all along the line
he demanded leave to carry his point on to the Privy
Council. The court found, however, that the matter in
dispute was under £5OO, and so a Privy Council
appeal was not allowable. The costs of this stubborn
officialdom came, of course, out of the estate.

The childlike and bland Rev.
H. Plow Kane, of Melb., is obi-
tuarised. He was mostly known
for his strict attention to company
business, during many years.
Nearly all his companies burst in
the boom, so that he really might as
well have concentrated his atten-
tion on the heavenly mansions.
The old gentleman fancied himself
a veritable Vanderbilt, but wasn’t
enough for the Davieses and
Muuroes, nor they for Beelzebub,
the god of this world.
“May the curse of the
Almighty rest on the electors for
not returning Macarthy O’Leary !”
was the malediition of a Vic.
R.C. clergyman after the last (old)
Moira election. Macarthy O’Leary
died soon afterwards. The Moira
electorate was split up into four,
Benalla and Yarrawonga being one
—the one in which the Curse was
uttered. Jack Brock was the first
M.P. Meeting with misfortune
he resigned, and Kerville was
elected in his stead, holding the
seat for just five days ! then Parlia-
ment was dissolved. Campbell
(Kerville’s former opponent) then
became Benalla’s M.P. Some 15
m mths 1 ter Campbell died. Then
came the notable Kennedy-Temple-
ton tie; Templeton tagged M.L. A.
to his name for three weeks, when
the election was upset. Six elections
in years. O, that Curse !
The fact that J. D. Young’s funeral, in
Sydney last week, was much more largely
attended than that of the other dead alderman,
Playfair, should furnish the epigrammatists with
a cynical remark or two concerning the eccen-
tricities of human estimates. The two men were,
in some respects, the very antithesis of each other.
Playfair was all sunshine and probably had not
a single enemy in the world ; Young was often
aggressive and vindictive. Everyone was Play-
fair’s friend ; but Young had a large and loyal
and powerful following. Both men were
intimately connected with municipal work,
and were understood to be fast friends. Playfair
was, in ’92, returned at the head of the poll as
M.P. for the democratic centre of West Sydney;
Young failed in his latter years to get into the
Assembly, and accepted an Upper House nomina-
tion. Playfair was successively a man-o’-wars-
man, a benevolent butcher, an alderman and
Mayor of Sydney, and an M.P. Young
fought in China against the Tae-pings, worked
as a Sydney wharf-laborer, made a pile as
a 'publican, became an alderman and Transit-
Commissioner, and M.L.O. Playfair was
English—an Essex native ; Young was Scotch.
Sylvester Browne “did” the Melbourne
Cup with enthusiasm, but he has probably lost a
good deal of the old desire for a stud. The Turf
was a field where the lucky mining speculator
didn’t strike it rich.
The German warship Sperber has taken
old Mataafa away from Nukunono (Tokelaus) to
Jaluit, Marshall Islands. Once there—the most
God-forgotten hole in the Pacific —the old warrior
will presently die of fret and soul-wearihess.

He stands about sft. 2in., wears a long beard, and

It is sad to remember that when David
Gaunson first beat the “ Dove of Ararat,”
M'Lellan, in the constituency named, and thus

Radical. He married money and the elder

Ex City-Solicitor Merriman, charged with
heavy official embezzlements, but not arrested
because of illness, died of brain disease last week
in a private hospital. The malady seems to have
been of gradual growth, and the case is one tend-
ing to show how difficult it is to properly gauge
the extent of human responsibility.
Lawyer Piddington’s eloquent protest
against working the coal-seams in and around
Sydney, lest scenic beauties have “their
wonderful glories of light and colour dimmed with
a pervading infusion of gloom ” is a reminder that
it needs a good deal of sense and natural ability to
study Keats and Wordsworth with impunity.
It is printed that the late Thomas Cooper
Makinson, a Cambridge 8.A., who arrived in
Sydney in ’37 as an Anglican clergyman and after-
wards “ ’verted ” to Rome, was the real author of
the Pastoral Letters for which the late Archbishop
Folding (whose private secretary Mr. Makinson
was) was so famous. He was 85 years of age
when he died near Sydney the other day, and
had been quite blind for about a decade.

a new book, to be called “ True
Stories from Australian History ”
—which will, he says, contain some
new and wonderful anecdotes of
William Charles Wentworth. In
all probability they will be spicy,
as, no doubt, they have their origin
in the papers of the late Lord
Sherbrooke, a sworn private enemy,
as well as a bitter political oppo-
nent, of W.C.W. Lowe’s hatred
for Wentworth was extended to his
family, and during the trial of the
divorce-case against a certain arch-
deacon, brought by his wife, nie
Wentworth, Lord and Lady Sher-
brooke found the “sinews of war ”
for the amorous church-dignitary.
By the way, G. B. Barton’s
“ Life of Wentworth ” will soon be
out.
M‘Kenzie Bowell’s mission from Canada
was one huge junket. He got through a dozen
“banquets,” a round of Melbourne “ Carnival
festivities, and a valedictory picnic. Then he had
a grand bilious attack and took himself back to
his winter snows, after mentioning that the con-
ference he came to attend will have to be held in
Canada, after all. Mr. Bowell was, in all pro-
bability, nothing but a business envoy from the
Canadian Pacific Railway,
“It was me,” answered Tommy Dodd,
from the Strangers’ Gallery of N.S.W. Parliament,
when the Speaker demanded who it was had called
a member of the House a liar; and Tommy’s
grammar was at once scored down against him.
But “me” in olden time was nominative as well
as objective. Dean Alford, a verbal purist and
author of several syntactical treatises, says “ me ”
is the natural conclusion of such a sentence as
Dodd used, and that he himself would adhere to
its use were it not for the ignorant outcries of
would-be English grammarians.
N.S. Wales Supreme Court Judges delight
in horsehair and man-millinery. Justice Owem
photo’d in gorgeous black and gold, is on view
at Freeman and Co.’s, George-street, Sydney,
with Chief Justice Darley close handy in
knee-breeches and buckles. In one of the
arcades cheek -by - jowl are Justices Stephen
and Manning in “ Queen’s Bench robes,” en-
deavouring to look solemn and wise ; alongside is
old ex-Judge Faucett in dittoes. Brother Foster
had his photos, done, but looked such a guy that
he bought them all up and burned them. Justice
Innes is said to be averse to photos.
I" The prize Pooh-Bah of Australasia is said
to be Policeman O’Rorke, of Otaki (M.L.). This
burdened officer, in addition to being an ordinary
constable, is clerk of the local court, clerk of the
licensing committee, registrar of electors, bailiff,
inspector of weights and measures, inspector of
factories, inspector of slaughter - houses, in-
spector of vehicles, inspector of noxious weeds,
agent for the Government Labour Bureau,
agent for the Government Trustee, inspector
under the Adulteration Act, registrar of dogs,
inspector of nuisances, inspector of licensed
houses, and ranger for the Acclimatisation
Society.
The Chesterfield of the South Seas is
undoubtedly Mr. Brigham, of Honolulu, now in
Sydney. Speaking of the young ex Queen Liliuo-
kalini, he says : “ The Queen is an accomplished
woman, can play and compose, and can entertain
with profuse liberality. She possesses, however,
personal qualities—failings they might more pro-
perly be called—which the gentleman interviewer
thinks utterly unfit her for the responsible duties
of a constitutional sovereign.” The beauteous
Queen Emma was also built that way. But the
graceful manner in which Mr. Brigham—yes,
Brigham ! —alludes to the royal “ failings ” is
simply charming.
Henniker Heaton usually invents his own
anecdotes, but the following bears some impress of
genuineness :—“ Scene, a rural post-office in Kent,
into which Mr. Heaton enters for six penn’orth
of stamps—neat. Postmaster chatty, and Mr.
Heaton not inaccessible. ‘ Things quiet ?’ asked
the visitor. ‘Very,’ replied the postmaster,
handing up a bundle of firewood and a jar of
treacle for a juvenile customer before proceeding
to balance his savings-bank sheet; ‘ and what is
more, we shall have it quieter if this ’ere man at
Canterbury has his way.’ ‘What’s that?’
demanded the interested stranger. ‘ Why, he
wants us to telegraph two words and call ’em one.
The wages here is bad enough already. Do
you think it’s fair to the taxpayer ’Enniker-’Eaton
should go for a ha’penny ?’ ”
The much-execrated “Bobby” Ross, ol
Taranganba (Q.) and Newcastle (N.S.W.), died at
the former place, a charming spot east of Rock-
hampton, and facing the Pacific. In his day, he
was a great horsey man, and held miles of rich
land on the coast, including that white elephant
of a bathing-place—Emu Park, bought from him
by a Griffith Government. Later on, he took to
mining—on private property. He found (or
founded) the famous Pigstye “ reef ” (?) on Taran-
Mr. H. R. HARWOOD.
ganba, which was to outvie Mount Morgan. Old
Bob could find gold everywhere; with a lantern
he could find it even at night. But no other
fellow could. And yet a lot of notable Sydneyites,
including even such level-headed people as
Ebenezer Vickery, sank thousands on the Pigstye,
which Ross duly pocketed.
Oar old friend Harwood’s pretty property
at Heidelberg, near Melbourne, which represents
most of old Uncle Catterrnole’s savings through a
long and industrious life, has, we grieve to hear,
gone to pay calls on reconstructed bank-shares.
This is “ La Cigale ” upside down ; it is not the
grasshopper but the poor ant that is out in the
snow these days. The man who has saved nothing
has no calls to pay, while the hard-working econo-
mist is put on the list of contributories and
bailiffed if he doesn’t settle up.
Edward Boyd Cullen, Under-Treasurer of
Queensland, who died at Brisbane of influenza on
Saturday, was a Scotch parson’s son, and had
been in Australia about 45 years. He was of old
a bank-clerk, and the first manager of Ipswich
branch of the B.N.S.W. Likewise, he was
Ipswich’s first Town Clerk, and, at one time, was
secretary to the wild, bushman-made North Aus-
tralian Club of that town. The N.A. was the
first club in Queensland, old Limestone, as Ipswich
was then called, being practically the business
capital of the province. Cullen went on a station
awhile and joined the Civil Service in ’6l, being
made Chief Treasury Clerk in ’62, and Under-
secretary in 77. The ex-secretary of a club in
which foregathered such men as Joshua Bell,
Wienholt, Macalister, Ferrett, Yaldwyn, George
Thorn, Faircloth, and everybody who was any-
body in the then moving country, couldn’t fail to
have a tremendous lot of political influence.

females emigrated from Japan to New Caledonia,
the Australias and the Pacific Islands. The bulk
of these went to Hawaii.

S.A.'Premier Kingston’s doctrine is that

The New Hebrides natives live ahead of
“civilisation.” When a man dies there, his pro-
perty is distributed among the tribe, his relatives
getting no more than utter strangers. On this
subject, read Max Nordau’s “ Conventional Lies
of Civilisation.”
The question as to whether or not private
landowners in N.S. Wales are entitled to the gold
beneath the surface should at once be taken out of
the hands of politicians and submitted for judicial
decision. If landowners own the gold they own
100 per cent, of it; if they don’t own it, their
claim is 0 per cent. The question is one for more
virile treatment than it can possibly get from
plastic politicians, accommodating party men and
landowners’ agents, and the sooner a legally
authoritative decision is arrived at the sooner the
public will know whether or not they have been
robbed and how best and most speedily they may
indemnify themselves.

The Brazilian insurgent leader, De Mello,
has proclaimed young Pedro, grandson of old Dora
Pedro, as Emperor of Brazil. Even if he gets his
throne, however, the monarchy will never again
be what it was. The nation has put its foot
through it once, and the dynasty which has once
been booted by its loyal subjects has lost its
mana.

As it was in the beginning, and is now, so it ever

Speaking of the “English” navy, the
Aequs emits a patriotic shriek “Probably not
even the French and Russian navies combined are
equal to it, above all in the skill of the officers and
the courage of the crews.” Pure theory ! What
the first great naval battle may bring forth no man
can foretell. And how the bold British tar de-
generates ! Not so long ago he was held good
enough for seven Frenchmen before breakfast;
now he is only equal to one Frank and a blessed
Muscovite or two.
Joseph Creer’s report to Premier Dibbs on
the quality of N.S.W. Labour Bureau bread ends
in a pitiably reprehensible sting. “ I find from
my experience,” writes the broken-down politi-
cian, “ that those who are ever ready to complain
of all they get and what-is done for them, are the
least deserving.” What is done for them ! Joseph,
like Lady Bountiful, appears to think that he is
the donor and not merely the paid distributor.
What if someone should remind him of what poli-
tical friends have done for him, and of how the
very persons he assumes such airs over are actually
taxed to pay him 1

The N.S.W. Labour-members, at a meet-
ing of 24 of their number, adopted resolutions
deprecating the very evident antagonism to them
of the late Labour Conference, and asserting their
right to devise their own plan of campaign inside
Parliament, as well as declining to accept all the
suggestions of the late Conference. In January
next, commencing on 17th, there is to be a confer-
ence of Labour-members from the various Aus-
tralian legislatures; and, several days later, the
Annual Conference of N.S.W. Labour Leagues
will be held. Meanwhile the N.S.W. Labour-
members declare that they will adhere to the
platform on which they were elected.
Major-General Hutton’s official declaration
that he found in the Vic. military forces a feeling
of goodwill towards their N.S.W. com pan ions-in-
arms, is a tin-whistle attempt to play Franco-
Eussian military amenities. Postmaster-General
Kidd might as well announce that on his visit to
Melb. he found theG.P.O. officials there animated
with sentiments of cordiality towards the proud
myrmidons who sort letters in his own edifice ; or
Inspector Fosbery might as well issue an official
bulletin to make known that the bobbies of Col-
lingwood entertained, on the occasion of his asking
them, fraternal sentiments towards their kin of
Woolloomooloo.
“The Englishman’s house is his castle,”
quoted Bruce Smith, in N.S.W. Assembly, in an
effort to prove that property was always sacred in
the eyes of the Anglo-Saxon. As a matter of
schoolboy history, Bruce Smith was wholly astray
in the application he made of the apothegm. The
Englishman’s house was not and is not his castle
in the sense of its being Property; indeed, he
can be thrown out of it very easily, where his only
claim to it is of that nature. But it was and is
his castle, inasmuch as when he has entered it
and closed his door his personal freedom is as
secure as was that of the occupant of a fortified
castle. Traill’s interjection, implying that
“ Liberty ” and not “ Property ” was the essence
of the apothegm, was understood by only a few, in
the House ; but it gave a cue which should have
awakened the intelligence of members.
Now that several general elections are
at hand in different provinces, the demand for
an automatic political egg-thrower again comes
to the front. When anywhere from two to fifty
people in the audience begin to egg an unpopular
candidate, the odds are obviously against that
candidate. What he wants, to enable him to hold
his own, is a large basket of ancient hen-product
and a machine with a-dozen iron hands which
will heave an egg with each hand twenty times a
minute, and thus keep up the politician’s end
of the combat while he goes on with his discourse
hidden behind an umbrella. Thk Bulletin has
seen the constituents egging their member often
enough ; what it wants now is to see the member
having a fair shot in return. There is a great
future before the man who invents an automatic
egg-thrower—say on the Maxim-gun principle.
In chronic diseases, medicines should be re-
storing and not debilitating in their action. The
strengthening and curative effects realised from
the use of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla sustain the repu-
tation of this remedy.

CHIEFLY ABOUT THE MERCANTILE CASE.


Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &c., in West Australia, which will be one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
offers exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
.£lO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well-known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J. Douglas,
who will be pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers where required.

Miss Ethel Stephen, daughter of Mr.
“ Seppy ” Stephen, of Sydney, was, at latest, to
marry, in England, a Mr. Bunn, or rather a gen-
tleman whose name was Bunn, but who changed
that somewhat unaristocratic cognomen for the
more euphonious one of De la Salle, “oncoming
into the property.” Mr. De la Salle is not, as
you might possibly think, connected with the
Boulevard St. Germain, but is a wealthy young
Scotchman.

Yice-regal private secretary Galton read
two papers at Sydney Women’s Literary Club,
last week. He told the dears —he-dears as well as
she-dears —that it was a fluky chance that he was
able to drop ’em anything ; but, some years ago,
he had been asked to write a series of lectures in
such simple style as the common people of the
East End of London might be able to understand,
and these papers were two of that series. “But
I don’t expect that you’ll understand them, for all
that, haw!” was what he didn’t say—at least,
not aloud.

# • *
Thus the Review of Reviews re Mount
Morgan gold-robberies
It has been suspected that a conspiracy existed among
some of the workmen to steal gold-laden charcoal and
gold amalgam. A Sydney detective was at last employed
who showed a skill worthy of Sherlock Holmes himself, or
of the hero of the Du Boisgobey novels. _ He obtained
employment in the mine, won the confidence of the
thieves, was made a partner in their plans, watched the
whole process by which gold was stolen, and followed it
until it was sold to the receiver. Mount Morgan gold is
of specially fine quality, and its mere purity will enable it
to be identified anywhere. The thieves, however, in-
geniously debased it by means of alloys. The euilty
employes joyfully reckoned their plunder at £2OOO a
month. Nine men have been arrested, and the Australian
law-courts have witnessed few more dramatic scenes,”
&c., &c., &c.
In the light of plain fact, the foregoing (a fair
specimen of the sensational stuff appearing in the
daily press) is absurd. The Sydney detective’s
skill consisted largely in gleaning the
maudlin utterances of a drunken man. Of the
nine men arrested, several have been already dis-
charged, and the evidence against three of the
others is of the flimsiest description. There is not,
so far, one tittle of evidence to support the assertion
that the gold was “ ingeniously debased,” and the
statement that Mount Morgan gold can always
be detected by reason of its purity was contra-
dicted in court by the acting-manager. Alto-
gether, the “ Great Gold Robbery ” of a hundred
glaring head-lines bids fair to turn out the very
smallest of small potatoes.

Mrs. Victor Cohen saw Oscar Wilde’s
play, “A Woman of No Importance,” in
London the other month, and a tale at-
taches. It was doubtful whether Australian
Eleven arrangements would bring Victor back to
headquarters i.e., the Cohen apartments in
London—on this particular evening when Mrs. 0.
and a lady friend were going to the theatre, but a
towzle-headed slavey was told the name of the
play and instructed to explain matters in the event
of hubby returning between 8 p.m. and 11 p.m.
He did turn up, as it happened. Also, he asked
the question usually put by married men on such
occasions. The slavey scratched her head dreamily
for five minutes. At last she scratched out a
vague remembrance of the message “If yer
please, sir, Mrs. Cohen told me to tell you she
was gone to spend the evening with a woman of
not much account.” Then Victor scratched his
head until Madame came home.

Scene, Newcastle, N.S..W. Tram crowded,
anc j rea( jy bo start. Stout old gentleman in the
corner looking out saw a Syrian woman running,
heavily-loaded. “Hurry up !” lie yelled, “hurry
U pi>’ The woman, panting, stumbled in.
“You’ve five minutes to spare,” said the genial
Q ] d p ar ty ( whose adipose frame fairly shook with
chuckles. The woman, young and pretty, only
smiled, tossed her dishevelled hair from her face,
stooped and took a comb from her bundle and
proceeded to arrange her long, glossy locks,
Sitting beside the old fellow (she greatly incom-
nioded him thereby) she handed him the hairpins
to hold, and flashed her teeth when he showed
reluctance. Then the whole car roared, and
the fat gentleman stepped out, very red, to the
salute of, “ Hurry away ! Hurry away ! You’ve
half a minute to get out of sight.”

* * *
Coroner Ghoul, of Melbourne, is getting
to be more and more afflicted with Cobbloquence
as he grows older. At every inquest he feels
called upon to make some eloquent remarks about
the corpse, and if the remarks don’t altogether fit
the corpse, that is the corpse’s own look-out. At
a recent inquest on James Irons, who blew out
his brains on an empty stomach one morning, it
transpired that the deceased was in the habit of
going to work at G. 30 and coming home at 9.30 for
breakfast. In the present instance, however, he
didn’t come home, because he was dead. Where-
upon Coroner Ghoul cleared his throat, and, after
some general remarks about death and the state of
continuing to be deceased after expiring, he
pointed out that poor Irons probably committed
the “rash act” for want of some refreshment to
keep him up, and directed the jury to find a ver-
dict accordingly. Which it did.
* * *
The more or less weekly leper comes in the
present instance from W. Maitland (N.S.W.). This
time the living death is a white girl, aged 16. The
local theory is that her case is connected in some
way with that of last week’s leper, or the leper of
the week before —the gangrened Asiatic who sold
lollies for many years at Newcastle, and shook the
germs from his decaying chest on to the confec-
tionery, which was afterwards eaten by the chil-
dren of the neighbourhood, _ Still this is only
theory; the one positive fact is that N.S. Wales
has got yet another leper.
# * *
Dean Cowper preached in St Andrew’s
Cathedral, Sydney, on Sunday last, among his
congregation being a contingent of the political
and social body known as the Active Service
Brigade. Beyond reading the red sheet called
Hard Cash during service the brigade made no
startling departure. The leaders hope, however,
that they will at an early date inaugurate an era
of smoking in church. Dean Cowper, who is in
his 84th year, abstained from alluding to the
contingent and discoursed, very ably for a man of
Ins years, on open topics,
* * *
A bush bankrupt, at the close of his exam-
ination in Sydney Insolvency Court, looked and
nodded significantly at the Registrar when that
official told him he might go. Some time after
the official came to know that the pantomime was
merely an invitation by the bankrupt to cross
over with him to a neighbouring hotel and take a
whisky.

sturdy agriculturist was struggling to make a

officials rolled into one, at .£5OO a year, and ran

well in the middle of his cultivation, and contrived
all sorts of irrigating conveniences. One day,
early in ’9O, a friend who visited him said, l>y waj
of congratulation I see you’ve got a well this

this time, rain or no rain.” He got plenty of
rain—in fact, 10ft. or 15ft. all over his place ! So

The North American Review holds up
the U.S. divorce system to ridicule on the ground
of its excessive laxity, and instances, as a proof,
the case of a lady who was recently granted a
separation, in one of the States, because her hus-
band didn’t wash himself. The Bulletin hopes
ere long to see this same kind of laxity in Aus-
tralia. So long as there is a water-supply avail-
able. either husband or wife should be able to get
divorced if he or she can prove that the other
party to the marriage-contract doesn’t take a bath
every day. The marriage-contract, in the case of a
dirty person, ought, in short, to be absolutely null
and void.
Further, The Bulletin would require, as a
preliminary to marriage, that both bride and
bridegroom should declare that he or she has
washed regularly in the past and will con-
tinue to do so in the future, and that
the offspring of the marriage, if there are
any, shall be kept strictly clean, and the house
scrubbed regularly, and the back-yard swept at
least once a week. Also, the proviso that the bride
and bridegroom are to wash themselves persis-
tently should be inserted in the marriage service,
and read out aloud as an essential part of the
ceremonial. And if the marriage should prove a
failure and the parties finally land in the divorce-
court, the case should be heard by a clean-washed
judge, and any juryman who hasn’t had a bath
that morning should be treated as an absent jury-
man and fined accordingly.
Also, The Bulletin would subject a dirty wit-
ness absolutely to fine or imprisonment ; and when
a contract is made between two dirty parties it
should be invalid, and when made between one
dirtv person and a clean one it should be valid or
otherwise, at the option of the clean person ; and
if the clean person elected that it should be valid,
then he or she, the clean person aforesaid, should
get six months’ gaol for being dirty in the mind.
Further, no debt due to a dirty person should be
legally recoverable; and when any case rests on
the testimony of a dirty witness which is contra-
dicted by the equally reliable evidence of a clean
witness, the latter should always get the verdict.
No doubt it will take a long time to educate the
public up to this standard, but in the meantime
an Act providing that too long an interval be-
tween baths shall be sufficient cause_ of divorce
would at least be a step in the right direction.

The latest retrenchment in N.S. Wales is
an economy in the matter of jurymen. When the
twelve men can’t arrive at a verdict they are
locked up in a cold, bare room, where
they_ are graciously permitted to sit straight up
all night and consider their verdict. A consider-
able saving in bedding is thus effected. The next
retrenchment should be in the matter of Judges ;
when they “ reserve their decision ” —which is
firactically the same thing—they should also be
ocked up in a bare and not over-clean apartment,
and kept there till they give judgment. Then
everything would be en suite. The prisoner is
locked up already, and the jury is looked up ; if
the Judge were confined in a special cell behind
the court-house and fed on skilly till the case
finished, things would at last begin to be
symmetrical.
• • *
“Was Glasson mad when he did the
Carcoar murders?” He was probably drunk—
with trouble. He had married a wife of whom he
was almost ridiculously fond, and whom he had
utterly misled as to his means, after the manner
of many men in love ; and seeing the pitiful
condition to which she had been reduced, and
full of remorseful apprehension as to their future,
he (perhaps taking his cue from the successful
bank-robbery, a few weeks previously, at Cargo,
in the Carcoar district) proceeded on his mad
mission—very likely intending robbery, and not
murder. “ I have been much struck,” writes Rev.
Geo. Walters, in a contemporary, “by this passage
from ‘Henry Jekyll’s statement’ of his case re
the murder of Oarew”: —
“ I declare at least, before God, no .man morally sane
could have been guilty of that crime upon so pitiful a
provocation ; and that I struck in no more reasonable
spirit than that in which a sick child may break a play-
thing. But I had voluntarily stripped myself of all those
b dancing instincts by which even the worst of us con-
tinues to walk with some degree of steadiness among
temptations; and in my case, to be tempted, however
slightly, was to fall. Instantly the spirit of hell awoke in
me, and raged." How curious are the coincidences of
fact and fiction!
It is the first step which costs. Moral: never
fall in love.

The libel-action, Speight v. the Age,
arrived at its sixty-fifth day with the distressing
news that Yic. ex-Commissioner Speight was a
“broker.” He was no longer able, explained his
counsel, to contribute a half-share towards the
support of a shorthand writer, and the evidence,
as far as he was concerned, could be taken down
in any way they chose. Thereupon, Syme’s
counsel thought that the Age proprietor would
prefer to pay for both halves of a shorthand
writer, and J udge Hodges said, “ I shall make no
order in that matter, nor will I refuse the plaintiff
a hearing because he cannot pay.” Impecunious
though Speight might be, he was still at liberty
to worry along with his suit somehow. The court
declined for the sake of auld lang syne to throw
him out on the 65th day. In much the same spirit,
a charitable cocoa-nut man will allow a long-
suffering customer to enjoy a few more throws at
the milky ones after he has parted with all his
cash—
They are seeking aid in England for the
maintenance of the Goodlet home for consump-
tives, at Picton, N.iS.W. “Englishmen, help us,
for they are nearly all incurables from the old
country who seek shelter in the home where they
can die in peace.”
• # #
At Honolulu, the other day, a few male
visitors made the sinfully popular arrangement to
“shout ” .£1 apiece for a giddy native dance, to
be performed by a certain number of damsels—as
many damsels as there were sovereigns to be put
in the pool—at the usual trysting-place. The full
complement of beauty was not provided, and the
girls that did come wore clothes, in addition to
which their dancing was quite decent and harm-
less. After a few minutes of this mild spectacle
two of the audience arose, in a state of manly dis-
gust. and went away. They had expected to be
properly shocked, they said, and seeing that the
Honolulu sirens were not carrying out the con-
tract this indignant pair of critics refused to cash
up their “quids.” The other gentlemen of the
party, grateful for small mercies, kept their
places and paid their money—in the interests of
true morality, one supposes. There is much to
admire in this little story. The picture of the two
baffled sensualists slinking away (and thereby
saving forty shillings between them) lives in the
memory. Moreover, it is comforting to think
that there are Honolulu sights to which any young
person might conduct his grandfather with im-
punity.

The barefaced behaviour of a high
dignitary up South in leaving his lawful spouse
at home and attending races, Govmentouse
garden-parties, and other public functions in the
company of his gold-washed friends (a new one
almost every time), is the subject of much com-
ment.
* • #
Some five years ago a Melb. undergrad,
stood before the notice-board of his university
wearing a “straw-decker” and academic gown.
Up walks a professor, not of the student’s pro-
spective profession, with : “Sir! You a student?
You’re a disgrace to the University. You deserve
rustication. I’ve a good mind to fine you £5 —
you young blackguard ! ” Shamefaced and humili-
ated, the youth rushed trembling for his “mortar-
board.” The other day he got his degree, and,
meeting the prof., he recalled the little incident of
87, finishing up with, “Professor Soanso, you’re
an infernal disgrace to this University, and I’ve a
good mind to teach you civility to the men you’ve
got authority over by punching your blanky
head.”

A pretty little woman, of Minnie Palmer-
ish appearance, has been roaming around Austra-
lia on the “ youthful innocence ” ticket. There is
no evidence of her having enjoyed a very good
time anywhere, and if she ever struck a comfort-
able billet as maid-servant or juvenile companion
to an old lady, this fragile flower (“Katie
Sinclair ”) didn’t stop there long, apparently.
Generally speaking, she was getting herself taken
up as a “neglected child” either by fatherly
policemen or a charitable institution. Magistrates
sent her to reformatories to save her from possible
ruin. Convent sisters took Katie in at the front-
door when she was overcome by a sudden dread of
falling into evil ways. The Salvation A.rmy
also gave Katie a good deal of protection from
the snares of this wicked world, and the hospitals
of Queensland accepted her frequently, under a
delusion that she was little Eva, about to join the
angels. But whatever home Katie found herself
in, she invariably bolted from, to the grief and
consternation of her new friends. They thought
she had gone forth, in her golden-haired sim-
plicity, to get drowned.
Kate’s latest public appearance was at South
Melb. Police Court, where a Harmy visitor sup-
plied them with historical facts concerning the
waif of tender years. Kate, it seems, is 30 at the
least, and so well informed for her age that she
corrupts other children in spite of her short frocks.
She speaks as a child, looks as a child, and feels as
a child, perhaps, but she can’t get away from the
experience acquired during a long and varied girl-
hood. An odd little spinster is Kate. Nature
must have intended her to cheat the evil eye of
the bald-headed sinner who gloats upon darlings
under sixteen. What are her plans for the future?
Has she any ambition to become the child-wife of
a kind Christian gentleman? Does she still de-
light to dress a dolly, or blush when little boys
ask her for a kiss? In the sacred name of the
Fair Sex, The Bulletin exhorts little Katie to
explain her aim in life. Also, she is advised to
repent of her youthful follies and become a bar-
maid. At present she is supposed to be staying at
the Abbotsford Convent for a year.
Why not have a doll auction after Flem-
ington races? It might be more payable than
the thoroughbred auction, which was a rank
failure. Put up the dolls, clothes and all, or in
bare poles. After all, this is what they go for,
but the market is terribly slack, and Auctioneer
Yuille might give it a fillip.

bootmakers in a similar way ; and would all the

MY PHILOSOPHY.
Launched in a cradle
Over life’s ocean
To land in a coffin.
What’s my philosophy ?
Haven’t a notion,
Let’s have a potion.
Dowell O’Reilly.

• * *
A hideous joke was played recently on a
Wellington (M.L.) police-sergeant. He was “on
jooty ” at the police-station when an excited
person rushed in and told him that a man was
walking along the Quay without any pants on.
Then the sergeant called three constables to assist
in arresting a dangerous maniac, and the four of
them fled to the Quay. “Fwhere’s the mad-
man?” said the sergeant in a tone of desperate
ferocity. The informant pointed him out—a
military person in a kilt and the essentials of a
Highland uniform—and then ran for his life.
There was an awful pause of 30 seconds or so, and
then the four policemen started after him on a
charge of insulting behaviour and followed him
for over a mile. They lost him at last somewhere
among the hills,

There was a Baptist Union meeting in
Melbourne, the other day, whereat a weary
fragment in a long black coat called attention to
the beautiful distinction between the big swindler
who says his prayers and the little swindler who
doesn’t. At least that is what The Bulletin
makes out of the following : “In troublous times
the Christian was vastly better off than the world-
ling. If they doubted this they should look (O !
look, dear brethren) at the long list of suicides,
abscondings and embezzlements almost daily re-
corded, and they would get a faint idea of the
terrible strain that tortured the victims of folly
who had no refuge in the storm, having no hope,
and being without God in the world.” The name
of the orator in the sable garment was Barber,
and Barber needs a severe lesson, in verse :
“Sharp as a razor” and “true as steel” are
most grimly ironical to the unfortunate man who
has to hack his beard off with a modern shoddy
razor. However, good Sheffield artisanscfostill exist,
and these are employed by Mr. W. Jno. Baker, of
3 Hunter-street, Sydney, to make his cutlery.
The razors so made with the name of W. Jno.
Baker etched on them, are sweet and clean, as
hundreds can testify from actual experience.
After shaving with one of these weapons the old
proverbs above quoted no longer lose their keen
application.
The attention of travellers and others i®
directed to the manifold comforts and elegancie®
of that snug and famous hostelry, the Imperial
Hotel, Wynyard Square, Sydney, which, at a
moderate tariff, provides an unapproachable
cuisine and elegant accommodation, more than
worthy of the best traditions of the most comfort-
able and quiet of old first-class Australian hotels.
For those who love a good dinner and a post-
prandial cigar, smoked under most perfect con-
ditions, the Imperial offers irresistible attractions.
Under the skilful superintendence of its host,
Walter Sidney, the Imperial is prospering greatly.
ihere is no greater foe to human happi-
ness than the disorders that spring from lack of
nerve vitality. The more, then, that we feel the
nervous organs of our bodies declining in strength
or diminishing in functional ability, the nearer
then we may be sure we are approaching to the
filial extinction of our lives. The causes that
lead up to this fading away of a man’s
existence, this waning of the pulse of life,
are many and varied. Mr, Rainsford, 7
Monour-st., Sydney, writes : “In order to get rid
of the remains of an attack of nervous debility
from overwork, I was induced, by reading your
advertisement, to try a bottle of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, and I found it acting so beneficially that
1 continued to use something like half-a-dozen
bottles. I am so pleased with its good effects
upon my system generally, that I shall recommend
it to any of my friends who may be suffering as I
was,” N.B.—Marshall’s Tonic Nervine is a
scientific discovery. It is not a haphazard pre-
paration resulting from a few minutes’ thought,
like many substitutes for it, but the outcome of
many years’ careful research into the cause of
weakness and anaemia in their many and varied
forms. It is a positive cure for weakness arising
from any and every cause, and can be obtained
from all medicine dealers. Do not be put off with
a substitute.
’Tis money makes the Law go round,
Which no one can deny ;
The price of Justice, neatly bound,
Has hitherto been high.
A suitor with a lengthy purse
Will seldom lose the day ;
-His privilege it is to curse
The man who cannot pay.
Yet Speight, the party versus Syme,
Can tell a touching tale :
His case along the track of Time
Proceeded like a snail
Until he had no money left
Wherewith to let ’em play.
Of billet, fortune, “ tick ” bereft,
The plaintiff couldn’t pay.
The Judge (may Heaven bless the name
Of Hodges) softly spoke—
“l see no legal cause to blame
The side that’s stony-broke ;
My solemn duty I must do;
I therefore rise to say
The Court will give a hearing to
This man who cannot pay."
A blessed fact to bear in mind—
The Law consents at least
To guard a lamb against the wind
If fairly be was fleeced.
O, Richard, raise your humbled eyes
And shout “ Hip, hip, hooray I’’
One learned Judge does not despise
The man who cannot pay.
Baptist Barber, shave your poll,
Shave your poll and keep it cooler ;
Don’t, because you’ve saved your soul,
Nag the boys who backed Camoola—
Put a bit upon Camoola.
Leave the worldling to his fate,
Let him smoke and swear and gamble.
Whilst adown the pathway straight
To the Realms of Light you amble—
Down the narrow' pathway amble.
Baptist Barber, since the boom
Tempted hawks to kill the pigeon,
Dark distrust has cast a gloom
Bound about the word “ religion "
True professional “religion.”
Them that offer hearty thanks
Unto Heaven for its mercies
Were the ones who, through the banks,
Dipped a hand in sinners’ purses—
Took the gold from sinners' purses.
Baptist Barber, kindly note !
Pious rogues have not absconded.
’Twas the dupe who cut his throat
When he wickedly desponded.
Weakly, wickedly desponded.
Saintly robbers snugly sing
Of a Lord who hath arisen ;
To the Cross they tightly cling
When they ought to go to prison—
Really ought to go to prison.
B iptist Barber, say no more
On this subject so distressing ;
Read your Bible as before,
But improve your style of dressing—
Ultra-holy style of dressing.
Barber, bear this fact,in mind.
Many “ Christians " who have tided
O'er their troubles are maligned
'Cause they never suicided—
Never, never suicided.

Medical.
ANNA RUPPERT
On POISONS.
Every Lady should Read this Carefully.
It has been said that some of vny preparations for the
skin contain poisonous ingredients, or rattier ingredients
that would he poisonous if taken internally and in snlfi-
cient quantities. This is not true of my SKIN TONIC,
which contains no such ingredients. I freely admit that
for certain stubborn skin diseases, like all successful
physicians and skin doctors, I do recommend stronger and
more effective preparations, some of which contain ingre-
dients which, if taken internally and in large quantities,
would be injurious and perhaps poisonous; hut when
applied externally and in small quantities, as directed,
they are never injurious, but always beneficial. With
the exception of my Dyspepsia Cure, which is purely
vegetable and consequently harmless, all my pre-
parations are for external use only, and w’here directions
are followed, it is absolutely impossible for any injurious
results to follow. It is well-known that many of the best
patent medicines and doctors’ prescriptions, for both
external and internal use, contain poisonous ingredients
in small quantities; but it does not in any way follow
that these medicines are injurious. On the contrary,
they are usually beneficial when properly used. Ask any
respectable doctor or chemist if this is not the case. J
give free consultations, and sell most of my preparations
direct to the customer, and for this reason, I sometimes
antagonise doctors and chemists, as well as my pro-
fessional competitors; but any fair-minded doctor,
chemist, or skin specialist will tell you that every
word I have said is absolutely true. Several prominenl
London physicians, who have become better acquainted
with me and my methods, have for some time sent their
“ incurable” eases to me and I have cured them. MY
CELEBRATED SKIN TONIC contains absolutely no
poisonous or injurious ingredient; is entirely harmless,
and always gives satisfaction. It lias for years had the
largest sale throughout the world of any skin lotion, and
is conceded to be the best and only satisfactory prepa ra-
tion for permanently and harmlessly cleansing the skin
and removing blemishes. After devoting my life to the
study and treatment of the skin, I would indeed he foolish
to advertise and sell, at great expense, anything but the
very best of remedies ; and especially anything that could
by any possibility be injurious. People who know me
also know that any preparation made or recommended by
I me for the skin can be used with entire safety and benefit.

LECTURE. Address

That BATHOS’ Powder is the best,

The first ballot in the Columbian Land
Distribution closes Monday next, Nov. 27, and
will be drawn three days later, Thursday, Nov,
30. The ballot comprises the Nambrok estate,
worth .£150,000, embracing 285 properties, the
chief prizes being valued at .£30,000, .£7OOO,
.£3OOO, <£2ooo, <£l6oo, 54 at £lO5O each, with
smaller prizes graduated down to 100 at £24each.
The agricultural lands of Gippsland, of which
Nambrok is an ideal type, need no recommenda-
tion. One share may be held by four persons
jointly, as will be seen on perusing the prospectus.
Next week’s ballot will be taken in the presence of
not less than 10 shareholders, and before press
representatives. Indeed, nothing that is not
aboveboard and genuine could reasonably be ex-
pected from such men as its supervisors ; P. P.
Labertouche a-nd Major C. M. Officer (Melb.);
Christopher Wade (Adelaide, S.A.); and Capt. J.
Whitney (Auckland, M.L.).
The horrorist of the Australian Star
spreads himself on a decomposed meat subject:—
There lies on the slab this morning at the South Sydney
Morgue a huddled mass of decomposed humanity un-
known and unclaimed. It is that of a man who ended his
days in the bush off Old Soutti Head Road, near Bellevue
Hill. No one knows how or when he did it, but,
miserable unto death, he betook himself from the
busy world, and far off from the city he trudged
along the road by Rose Bay, and while no eyes
hut those of his Maker were upon him he crossed the
fence and entered the hush. A few minutes later a re-
volver-report sounded through the hills, but none heard
it hut the birds in the trees. Then all was quiet, and,
perhaps, a few minutes later some resident in that locality
drove by unmindful of the unfortunate being who lay
dead but a few yards eff. The body lay there, the sun
shone on it, and the rain beat heavily into the upturned
face. The birds hopped around, maybe upon it, but no
fellow-being noticed it until yrsteiday afternoon, when
John Davidson, a resident of 62 Landsdown-street,
Surry Hills, came upon the horribly-decayed body.
The face had wasted away, and fallen by the bone, the
dark clothes covered the remainder of the body. By the
side lay the instrument of death, a loaded five-chambered
revolver, with one chamber only discharged. There the
destroyer and the destroyed lay aide by side. There was
no one to tell the tale when or why the deed was done.
Close by lay the tattered and weatherworn scraps of the
Evbning Nkws of October 23, and most likely that was the
date of the suicidal act.
Base deduction ! One’s first impression was that
it was all going to pan out as a St. Jacob’s oil
advt. Apart from this, the most striking feature
about the story is the statement that the eye of
God was upon him as he got over the fence. It is
a good specimen of cheap report,erese. Neverthe-
less, it might, perhaps, be spread out a little with
advantage:—
It was night when a lonesome atom of lost humanity
turned his back upon the city with its sin and bankruptcy
and pearl-powder and its callous heartlessness. He
laughed a hollow laugh in two syllables as I e went, in
his broken boms, up William-street—a dreadful laugh like
the caci hination of a demon. The rain paltered heavily
on the trees which stretched gaunt, bristling limbs sky-
ward like the frozen hair of a lost soul, A girl in red
stockings lied in affright as she heard the dreadful mirth
of the deceased. Down by Rnshcutters Bay the wanderer
silently look a hone out of an ash-box, a'd as be went
along ite deposited it on the doorstep of a palatial mansion
as a mute protest against the inhumanity of man. There
was no m at on the hone. Later on, at the top of a hill,
ust below the lordly residence of Mister James of the
S.M. Hbkald, lie paused and lookid h ng and earnestly,
out of his unshaven countenance, on the angry' waters of
the hay. Then he howled tw ice and got over the fence,
busting the top wire as he went, and sat down wearily
beneath a tree. “Tins place," he remark'd in
Latin, “will do as well as any other. 1 will pre-
sently be disintegrated and resolved into tny con-
stituent elements, and will take wing into the
empyrean.” As he spoke he wrung the water
out. of his coat-tail, and then drawing a revolver he
fired several times at his head and was no more. His re-
mains were walked on for several months by various
animals, and were looked at by cows with that long,
earnest, unseeing stare which is charaoteiistic of the
animal. Then one bright summer dav, John Jones, of
9S7J Macqnarie-street North, up one stair, second door on
the right, passed that way, unsuspicious of evil, and met
with the awful spectacle of the deceased. He was re-
moved to the Morgue by Constable Brown, whose sagacity
in keeping to windward of the remains, and rushing them
into town on a w'heelharrow ticketed “dynamite,” in
order to keep off the thoughtless multitude, will, doubt-
less, be recognised by the authorities.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Murdoch says now what The Bulletin said
years ago, that “ sufficient credit has not been given for
the great improvement in English cricket in reviewing
the performances of the Australians." Twelve years ago
there was no life in the bowling and fielding of the
Britishers, and the enthusiasm shown by the Australians
came as a'revelation. A professional could be dis-
tinguished from an amateur by his slovenly run and pick-
up at that time, whereas the pro. of to-day is almost as
active on his legs, and even quicker with liis hands than
the average Australian performer. Cricket is a much
more profitable game for him than it used to be—the
“princely patron ” element has stirred him up.
* 9 *
Last season’s English cricket-figures show that
the amateurs as a class are far better batsmen and far
worse bowlers than the professionals. Of the 26 leading
batsmen 20 are amateurs ; of the 17 leading bowlers two
only are amateurs.
• * *
Turner says that Ooningham quarrelled with no
one on the recent tour, nor did he ever behave culpably.
Coningham played the fool now and again on the cricket-
field, but his eccentricity was always pardonable, and
throughout he was a strict teetotaller.
* 9 *
Sydney cricketers sura up the bad members
of a certain team:—“One man was nearly always
beastly drunk ; another was a manageable beast when
drunk ; a third was an unmanageable beast when drunk ;
a fourth, an unmanageable beast, drunk or sober.”
# * »
Barrett (5.M.0.C., and Dr. “Jack’s” brother)
is a certain intercolonial man, with two scientific scores
of 95 and 84.
* » •
Victor Cohen says the receipts of the Australian
cricketers, in the English tour, totalled £9OOO.
tt * *
Cricketer Murdoch declares that the great need
of the late Australian Eleven was a fast bowler. A
greater need still—a few batsmen who were not so fast.

thing apropos. And a big English county club was just
on the verge of making a proposal to Graham when—
something happened.

“ Tam O’Shan ter,” the sporting writer of Tas.
Mail, claims to he the only Australasian scribe who
named Tarcoola among the probable C-up winners of
Melb. This it was, probably, that caused an unfortunate

share in Tarcoola.
# 9 *
J. D. Young, the just-deceased Sydney muni-
cipal magnate, was only another instance of how hopeless
it is for an outsider to try and fight the bookmakers,
J.D. was supposed to be one of the knowing men behind
the scenes, and a small crowd would tout him round the
ring to see what he was hacking. At the Driving Park
and Carrington Ground, when in their hey-day, he was
all-powerful as being “in the know,” but he left off like
the rest of them —thousands to the bad.

* * *
Last week the Sydney police raided one “ tote ”
with much pomp and circumstance, and ran in a large
crop of defendants, who were duly brought before the
court. Then, after one case had been heard, and
defendant discharged, the charges apparently faded
away, and nothing definite has been heard of them since.
The defendant in question, it appears, had been captured
on a warrant which spoke of him as illegally “ haunting
and resorting” to the gambling - house in question,
whereas the Act didn’t use the words “ haunting and
resorting,” but employed the expression “frequenting,"
or something to that effect, and this discrepancy burst up
the case at the very beginning. There was absolutely
no difference in the meaning of the words ; in each case
it was meant to be conveyed that the defendant attended
the premises, and hung round, and was often seen there,
and made the place an habitual resort. But the civil law
of Australia long ago gave up the habit of considering
matters of simple justice, and went astray after points of
order and technicalities which only serve to pervert the
course of equity and make the code % mockery.
* * *
According to Bathurst (N.S.W.) Times, some
turf sharps took a bay horse from Sydney to an up-
oountry race-meeting, dyed him jet black, entered him
in a false name, and won with him everything they started
for.
• * #
Influence is being used with a Bart, and a Q.O.
to have the suspended jockey, Jimmy Hayes, re-instated
in his high place in the saddle.
• • «
Dear Bulletin, — Excuse me re Bridesmaid’s 19
foals. Trenchant, foaled in ’9O, was Bridesmaid’s 15th ;
so that, if she foaled every year since, she has but 18,
unless twins were in it. The mare shows the highest
colonial stud-book record for fruitfulness. Old English
records name several equal to our highest, but I have only
heard of two mares exceeding 18 foals.—Yours, &0., Horse
* • •
It is related that one of the big prize-winners in
recent Tatt. sweep (Melb. Cup) was a young husband who,
having started matrimony on a somewhat too extensive
scale, sold his furniture a while ago. When his bride
heard the glad tidings she beamed upon Adolphus and
said she always knew he would make a lot of money as
soon as he got the chance. The ordinary husband who
buys a ticket is merely informed that he was a fool to
gamble like that instead of paying the baker.

Despite the falling-off on Derby and Cup days,
the V.R.C. railway returns for Steeplechase day show an
increase of £llO9 on the previous year.
* * ♦
The small head has quite gone out of date’for a
racer. The moderns would be termed “fiddle-headed
brutes,” by scribes of yore.
# * •
A Melb. policeman curses his luck : he was told
to “ raid ” the sweep in which he had drawn Tarcoola.
* * *
Lathy, flat-chested, consumptive-looking Billy
Maher lias fi led out, in the States, into an list. man.
The other day he beat La Blanche, “the Marine,” and it
is now talked of pitting him against “ Buffalo” Costello.
* # *
One W. W. Naughton, who sends from the
States a monthly letter on Yankee sport to a Sydney
weekly, writes in all seriousness that Jim Stanbury was
afraid to tackle the Yankee scullers. As a matter of fact,
the Australian refused to row mountebank races “ with a
turn ” against fourth and fifth raters like Petersen. Hence
the gall.
« * »
East bowling is dangerous to batsman and
bowler. Latest victim, P. C Charlton, who is suffering
from internal strain, and is forbidden to bowl at all this
season. He last played in an intercolonial - and nearly
fainted at the crease. The great Lehmann broke down in
a similar way. Turner, called by Englishmen “The
Terror,” is, tor hardiness alone, a living wonder among
bowlers.
* * *
A ticketless wag, travelling from Melbourne to
the last (say Bargowlah) Cup-meeting, induced the
train-guard to pay his fare on the strength of a
plausible yarn that Malvolio was entered for the Cup
under another name, and that his coat had been
so altered with dves as to positively conceal his
identity. “It was a real dead snip if the jockey didn’t
squeak.” He was “in the know, and they were going t
break the books and win tons of money." The guard
bit, and, besides paying for the ticket and giving up his
own sleeping-berth, shouted a bottle of brandy. He quite
made up his mind that Speight wouldn’t boss him any
more. “ It’s a dead bird, you say ? ” “As dead a cop as
ever was; only don't breathe it to a soul—it it gets about
we’re ruined.” “Is he in at a light weight?” “Yes,
old ohap, that’s where we’ve got ’em. Win, my boy! it’s
the deadest snip in the world if the jockey don’t squeak.”
When the train reached the Bargowlah, it was arranged
that they should meet-on the course half-an-hour before
the Cup was run and plank their cash. At the appointed
time the guard, feverishly excited, rushed up and found
his friend sobbing bitterly, “I’m ruined! ruined!!
ruined!!!” “•Ruined?" “Yes." “ What’s happened?”
“ The jockey * squeaked.’ ”

Ex-champion sculler Ned Trickett is now bridge-
keeping for the N.S.W. Customs on the Murray.
* * •
Dear Bulletin, —Like horse-yarns? Here’s
one. N.Q., in seventy something-eight, I think. Reef-
ing—good show (wish I had it_ now). Used to
pack tucker from Cooktown with own horses.
Doughboy nice horse fiddle-head—pensively pen-
dulous under-lip—sweet, meditative smile—more sense’n a
man—eat anything—chase you for bread and jam. One
day Warden (Hill) down from Byerstown to our claim—
feed, of course—bush hospitality—(going bung now)—
Doughboy outside listening to conversation —good listener
—great ear-capacity. Warden sitting with back to entry
—large slice bread and marmalade—talking earnestly—
elbow on table—Doughboy twigs that slice—stretches
neck about six feet—that lip good for two more—snaps it
—yell and jump from Warden—complacent smile on
countenance of horse (Unreportable language
of Warden). Never mind, try again. Another slice-
bread and mustard—lots of latter—invitingly held up
while continuing talk. Doughboy repeats performance—
a surprised and pained look takes place of former smile —
big tears roll down cheeks. Never take the choicest
morsel from Warden after that. Lie? P’raps so. Told
it so often, I dunno.—The Palmer.
• * *
Mr. Alf, Lawler, a Sydney sport just returned
to Australia, saw Corbett box in America. “He is a
bigger man than Choynski—in fact, about the same size
as Jackson, and is a dashing boxer, with a style recalling
that of the little champion, Griffo.”
• • •
The latest billiard-match between Ives and
Roberts produced unique play. Roberts, 945 points ahead
on Wednesday afternoon, was, when the game finished
same evening, 115 behind Ives! When Ives scored so
marvellously in London with the balls jammed in the
corner, he was put down as a mere trickster; but breaks
of 651, 515, and 395 in one evening at. ordinary English
billiards proves him to be a player without a rival.
# # #
A cricket-match, W. G. Grace’s Eleven v. W.
W. Read’s Eleven, played after the close of the legitimate
season, was probably unique in the circumstance that
Grace and Read each made top score for his side—l3s
and 112 respectively.
Melbourne Agk strongly agitates for legalised
totes.
• • •
The first Melb. racecourse, Batman’s Hill, was
straight. The present one is pretty crooked.

The Argus says pedestrianism is dead through
the rogueries practised, and then goes on to argue, by
mplication, on the snow-white purity of horse-racing 1
Some 30,000 frozen Australian hares were
delivered in one shipment in London lately.
They are described as excellent. Most of them
were retailed as “ best Norfolks” at ss.
Zola having made Albert Edward a leading
character in “Nana,” Tummy gave him a very
cool reception on his recent visit to England.
At Uardry, Gulgong, N.S.W., is a year-old
sheep with two mouths, one in the orthodox
position, the other in the ear. “ The lower part of
the ear forming the jaw opens and shuts in the
ordinary manner when the sheep is feeding, and
contains two teeth.” Wanted, a stump-orator with
two mouths. He would be worth millions.
At Merriwa (N.S.W.) a drover has been
fined £l, under an old Charles 11. statute, for
travelling sheep on a Sunday. The N.S.W.
statute law declares that a drover of sheep must
travel his animals six miles a day, Sunday or
otherwise, unless weather forbids. If he doesn’t
he is fined. He is fined either way.
The Queensland unionist shearers’ strike'
prisoners, gaoled for three years on a charge of
conspiracy, have served their sentences, arjfl were
last week released. Although the men Were not
landed at Brisbane until 9.30 p-.'m., some hundreds
of their mates were present to welcome them to
freedom, and to bore them with speeches. One
portion of their sentences required the finding of
sureties of good behaviour lor another year, but
the 26 sureties necessary could have been supplied
several times over. Ever since their incarcera-
tion, an assistance-fund for their benefit has been
accumulating. To the wives of the two married
men among them, <£2 a week has been paid all
along; besides which something like <£2500 stands
to the credit of the fund, which is to be divided
among them. The men, by petitioning for
“mercy” at the instigation of Col. Secretary
Towzer, could have obtained a “pardon” long
ago, but they declined to adopt an appellant
attitude.

The bed that Thackeray died on was sent
to his old school, the Charter-house. The head
gown-boy has the privilege of sleeping on it.
According to the laws of officialdom, all
sovereigns are semi-ecclesiastioal personages. But
it would be difficult to accept, say, Albert ltd ward
as a semi-ecclesiastic.
At Caiwarra station, near Hungerford
(N.S.W.), during the recent shearing, the cook
had as pets some fifty or sixty pelicans. Walking
down to the bank of the lagoon, he would whistle,
and, in a few moments, be surrounded by an open-
mouthed mob of hungry birds. So tame were
they that they would take bones from his hand
and reach up and pick them off his shoulder.
The two Dutch men-of-war despatched by
the Netherlands-India Govt, from Batavia to
seize the Australian pearling parties alleged to be
“scooping” the Dutchmen’s pearl-shell, are the
Sumatra and the Pontianak. If the former vessel
is the same Sumatra that was stationed at Dutch
New Guinea in ’75 there is little chance of her
catching the Australians. She was a paddle-
wheel steamer, and capable (in smooth water and
with a four-knot current in her favour) of steaming
six knots an hour.
Thirty years ago, as G. R. Sims reminds
his readers in London Referee, a specially ram-
pageous boy was, by common consent, set down as
“ a young Radical.” The term indicated a pro-
bably disreputable manhood terminating on the
gallows. Unless the young Radical repented and
became a juvenile worm he would grow into an
adult Radical, for which class of person there was
absolutely no salvation in those days. Every
anxious parent prayed that his boy might be a,
dear little Submissive Tory and a credit to his
country.
Fifty per cent, of “plucks” in N.S.W.
junior exam, this year. The examiners in every
department say that they have never had a more
miserable set of papers to correct. Two reasons,
amongst others, for this. First, the fond idiocy
of the parents who send their darlings up as soon
as they have been promoted from a feeding-bottle
to a spoon and finely-chopped meat. Secondly,
the incompeteney of the teachers. One, a junior*
and-senior tutor, who teaches geology at a ladies’
college, actually asked, the other day, what obsi-
dian was.
Before undergoing operative or other
treatment for Piles, consult Mr. A. Sharp on the
new and successful “ Cannalin ” treatment, 257
George-st., close to Bridge-st., Sydney.*
Dear Bulletin, —Horace Greeley says that
swearing is a Christian virtue, because it so
admirably answers the purpose of getting rid
of emotions. Bishop Goe, in his lecture on
the Continuity of the Church of England, says
(in re Henry VIII.) that “that man reads the
pages of history to little purpose who has not
observed how often God in providence makes use
of a bail man to accomplish his own wise pur-
poses.” If these assertions are correct why
endeavour to repress any alleged “bad habit?”
It may be a Christian virtue in disguise. Why per-
secute poor Matthew Davies and his fellow land-
boomers? They may have been specially sent by
Providence to show the folly of living miserably
and saving your cash for the proverbial rainy day.
Why hang Makin and try to hang Mrs. Th waites?
Their purpose may have been to bring the case of
the unaccountable infant prominently before the
public. Why repress anything or punish any-
body ?—M.B.H., South Yarra.

A Love Story.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ He went up-country and was reported dead,”
said the traveller to his mate as they sat down on
their swags.
“ He was reported to have been drowned while
trying to swim his horses across a billabong. His
girl broke her heart —and mended it again ; then
he turned up alive, and drier than ever, and
married her, and broke her heart for certain. And
—she died.”
He spat in the dust and scraped it impatiently
with his foot.
“She was—she was an old sweetheart of mine,”
he said, speaking low and as if to himself.
He rested his long arm listlessly on his knee and
absently scraped across in the dust, between his
feet, with the blade of a pocket-knife.
“Ah, well —nevermind. . . The billy’s boil-
ing, Joe.”
HENRY LAWSON.
Some appropriate Sydney sign-boards:
“ Lugg, dentist; ” “ Whale, oilman ; ” “ Mrs.
Child, midwife;” and years ago the legend on a
city hotel was, “The Rising Sun, by Will Shine.”
Who objected to the statement that Eng-
land is 15,000 miles from Australia “seeing that
in a straight line it is not more than 10,000 miles ?”
A straight line is through the earth and is not
more than 7000 miles.
Abbd Duclos told a circle of ladies that an
honest woman could listen to anything, and then
went on to tell them a pretty story. He followed
with a better, but—“ Hold,” said one, “we are
not so honest as your reverence supposes.”
r I he daily press denounces the proposal to
make N.S.W. State education absolutely free;
but it doesn’t denounce free postage of news-
papers. Free postage of newspapers ought to be
suppressed as it “educates the people beyond
their station in life.”
Maitland Mercury (N.S.W.), we see, is
to appear as a daily evening paper on January 1
next. There will also be a weekly issue of larger
dimensions every Saturday. This, under all the
circumstances, strikes The Bulletin as one of
the neatest newspaper moves on record.
The young King of Spain is only getting
about .£200,000 per annum. But if he sticks to
business and gets around to the throne early in
the morning, and only takes twenty minutes for
lunch, and doesn’t knock off before dark, there is
no reason why he shouldn’t have his salary raised.
Dear Bulletin, — Re Jim McCormick’s
alleged record for long-distance driving. I must
say that, after 13 years coaching in several of the
provinces, I have never known a man to do the
work George Raynor accomplished while driving
between Manna Hill (S.A.) and Wilcannia (N.S.
Wales). The distance out and back was 520
miles, and he did one round trip every week, or,
rather, as near the week as possible, but as a fall
of rain in that country brings coaching to bullock-
pace, George often got off his coach at one end
only to have a meal and mount the box for the
return trip; oftener still, he met the return coach
a few miles out from either end, and simply ex-
changed coaches and turned back over his terribly
monotonous track. Yes, I think Raynor’s work
for three years without a spell, will take all the
beating Jim M‘Cormick or anyone else can give it.
While on the subject of records, I may say that I
sat on the box and drove five-horse teams for 107
hours without sleep or stop, save to change horses,
which, I fancy, establishes a record in its way,
and I may add that in all that weary time I only
covered 310 miles. So bad was the track that I
took 10g hours to get over one of our best stages
of 25 miles, while many an hour was spent in
walking beside the horses—as good as ever
worked when feed was plentiful—and urging
them on, bullock-fashion. After this and
many similar experiences, I find it difficult to
believe that Jim M’Cormick gets over 580 miles
every week. If he does, it must be on a macada-
mised road, for on every bush-track I have ever
Polled a rein over, a fall of rain, no matter how
slight, makes hours of difference in a trip, and
thus sadly interferes with the monthly average.—
Swingle Bar.
