M'Burney, Wallis & Co.'s Report.
Wool.—At our third sale of the season, held yesterday,
the attendance of buyers-French, German and English—
was extremely large, and competition very strong up to a
level of prices slightly under those ruling last week for
fleece wool, but bellies, pieces and locks are just as eagerly
competed for. A feature of the sales is the great demand
for what are commonly called “ star lots”—that is, lots of
three bales and under. Owing to owners’ idea of’ values
being somewhat above the market, a few of the prin-
cipal lots were withdrawn, but as the difference is not
material, no doubt business will result. For 36 bales
greasy fleece marked HB/Fernglen, from Gundagai
7d was obtained. This was a fine free wool, nicely
skirted ; 34 bales Boree, from Goulburn ; JQ over Coolac
9 bales, and JG over Middle Station, 7 bales from Yass’
rea'ised the same figure ; 19 bales marked 4/JG, from
Wagga, fetched s|d-this lot was very heavy, wasty, and
not sufficiently skirted. A nice line marked WC/G, from
Gundagai, consisting of 30 bales, was passed in, the bid-
ding not reaching owner’s idea of value. AMD and
D/Wagga, from Deepwater and Wagga respectively,
realised 6£d per lb., the former being a little wasty, and
the latter touched with grass seed and skirty. For cross-
bred of medium quality 7fd per lb. was obtained for a
line from Rockley marked JS over R, and the same
figure for 2 bales WC/G. Scoured wool sold very well, a
further line of the well-known PR wool from Tumut
realising IOJd for the first skin, 9Jd tor second skin, and
12d for the fleece.
Sheepskins.—With the exception of green skins, all
classes are eagerly competed for, best full-woolled selling
up to 4|d ; superior, 5d ; medium, 3|d to 4d ; pelts, 3id
per lb.; green skins, to 52d for tops, 30d to 40d for
medium, and 9d to 15d for pelts.
Hides.—As the sales progressed the demand improved
for all classes, extra-heavies selling to 3Jd ; heavies, 2d to
2Jd; medium and light, IJd to lsd ; dry, to 2d per lb.
Tallow.—Market without alteration ; prime mutton
£23 to £23 55.; mixed, £22 15s to £23 ; medium, £22 to
£22 12s 6d ; beef, best, £22 to £22 10s; medium and
inferior, £lB to £2l per ton.
Horns.—Large, £1 to £llos ; medium, 10s to 17s 6d ;
tips and small, from 2s per 100.
Bones.—Common £1 10s to £2 ss: knuckles, £2 to
£2 10s ; shanks, £4 to £4 12s 6d.
Hoofs. —£2 10s per ton.
Hair.—Horse, 9d to 13Jd ; cow. 6d to lOd ; stump. 2d
to 7id per lb.
Marsupial Skins.—No change to report, with the
exception of rabbit and faulty skins all lines are well
competed for. Large grey kangaroo, 40d to 52d;
medium, 20d to 30d ; small, Id to 18d ; red, large, 15d to
24d; small and medium, Id to 14d ; wallaroo, Id to 18d;
wallaby, swamp. Id to 14d, scrub ; Jd to 14d ; rock, Jd to
2d each. Rabbit, id to 6d per lb. Opossum, grey, 6s to
10s; seconds, 3s 6d to ss; rough and rumped, from 6d
per dozen.
Sydney, 17th November, 1893.
“ Com© and hay© a drink ” is what is now
heard in our streets, but how often do you get
any good out of the decoctions retailed in the
majority of the drink-shops? Next time you use
this expression just slip into Stedman’s, or the
Fresh Food, and get a cup of Fry’s Cocoa.
Frank McDonald, a shearer staying at
Melb. Federal Coffee Palace, while in a state of
somnambulism climbed out of a seventh-storey
window, and swarmed up a spout on to the roof,
an apparently impossible feat. He spent 19 hours
on the roof in his trousers only, and finally
attracted attention by throwing bricks into the
street. Had M'Donald slipped whilst climbing
he would have dropped plump 100 feet. He said
he “ had been drinking a little.”
The epidemic of young girl suicides con-
* tinues in Maoriland. Also, despite the ghastly
consequences, they almost all stick to the one
poison—a lot of match-heads swallowed in a cup
of water, last mail two more of these affairs
arerepqrted. Theperformersareagedlsandl6years
respectively, and at latest advices they were both
in the hospital and recovering in great agony.
Suicide by eating matches is generally unsuccess-
ful, for the sensation which follows is very like
that of trying to balance boiling water on the pit
' of the stomach, and the patient has to yell at the
j top of her voice long before death is anywhere
J near. The profound ignorance of the Maoriland
girl suicide is her most conspicuous feature. What
she wants is to die in her best white frock and be
found nicely laid out on her little white bed with
a restful smile on her features and a red, red rose
in her waxen hand. And if she did manage to
die by swallowing boxes of matches she would
only do it after long and dismal agonies, in the
course of which she would prance all over the
house, and ruin her frock by trying to force her-
self up the chimney, and by rolling over and over
on the coals, and in the end she would probably
set herself on fire and then rush out screaming
into the back premises, and cast herself head-long
down a steep place into the fat-barrel. There is
nothing poetic about being discovered dead,
distorted, and covered with soot, and huddled up
shapelessly among the grease and kitchen bones,
and the sentimental girl-suicide might make a note
of it.
To Non-Subscribers and Distant Readers
The Bulletin’s Xmas Number, to be published
December 16, at this paper’s usual price, 6d., will
shine like a star. It will not be a fat hunk of
paper stained with English stories, bought abroad
for ss. a page, shared with scores of other papers
throughout the world, and backed up with yards
of stolen matter and a garish foreign-printed pic-
ture of a girl nursing a kitten.
The Bulletin Xmas Number will be absolutely
original, both as regards its literary matter and its
pictures, which latter will be contributed by
the leading artists of Australia. The literary
contents will comprise contributions in prose and
verse from over 40 old and new Australian
writers, and there will not be one dull line from
first to last. The number will be the best and
biggest and most original sixpenny-worth of read-
ing published by any news-magazine in the world.
Single copies of The Bulletin’s Christmas
Number will be mailed to any Australasian address
outside N.S. Wales for Bd. in stamps; to any
English or foreign address for a remittance of 9d.
in stamps sent to the office, 24 Pitt-street. Sydney
N.S. Wales.

A N.S.W. Legislative Council Committee has
found that the Costa Rica Packet claims against the
Netherlands-India Govt.—by Capt. Carpenter, £10,000;
owners, £lO,OOO ; subordinate officers and crew, £5OO0 —
are reasonable and moderate, and should he pressed.
Another thing that needs pushing is the N.S.W. whaling
industry, seeing that at least one vessel entering Sydney
Heads of late has bumped against a sperm-whale.
# * •
Dr. Cullen, M.P., in the N.S.W. Mining on
Private Property debate, stood up as the landlord’s cham-
pion, evidently in ignorance of the fact that such a law
has for 20 years in Victoria worked for the benefit rather
than tiie injury of property-owners. _ The present position
of Bendigo is largely due to the right to mine on and
under private lands.
# » •
After a year’s silence the scheme to carry out
big works of various kinds in New Caledonia has been re-
vived in Sydney. If the Governor of that island were
aware of half the statements made relative to certain
concessions he would sit right up.
« » •
Dowell, M.P., says if greater scope was allowed
private enterprise there would be no unemployed. Let’s
see—isn’t Dowell the man who fought for a charter for
Abigail’s bank? And hadn’t he a fist in the Deepwater
tramway? Has it been laid, and if it hasn’t, what penalty
has beeh paid by Dowell and his friends?
# • •
“ Tattersall ” has consented to supervise the
ballot for the distribution of the properties of the Equit-
able Building Society of Q. —which announcement should
“make assurance doubly sure” since the names of the
promoters of the scheme were of themselves a sufficient
guarantee. We are assured that the Equitable will derive
no direct profit from this distribution, _ its aim being
merely the realisation of assets otherwise inconvertible in
these times. The share-list will close on the last day of
this month, by which time it is expected that the re-
quisite capital will be over subscribed. Messrs. Pierce
and Co., of Pitt-street, Sydney, are central agents for
N.S. Wales, and are empowered to issue scrip and supply
information.

* • •
All up with Ooolgardie now—a Sunday paper
has described it as a magnificent field 1
• • •
The Ooolgardie people estimate that at least
30 people have died on the drought-cursed road which
leads to their haggard El Dorado out in the wilderness.
Waterholes (merely sinks of blackened mud) average
about 15 mips apart. The camel is almost the only
Providence known in these parts, and many a dry pros-
pector thinks it the most beautiful animal on earth when
its gaunt hump looms up between him and the setting sun.
• * *
The Randt field, S.A., has the largest-salaried
mining managers in the world. The pick of America
have already gone there, and more are going. One man
is getting £6OOO, another £5OOO, a third £4OOO, and
several £2OOO and £3OOO a year. A big offer has come to
a man in N.S. W. with an American reputation.

witch, are now fitting out a second vessel, the Helen. By

sleepy Sydney may begin to move.
# * •
The deadly-lively village of Port Macquarie
struggles in the throes of a gold discovery, and sluicing
proceeds at Shelly Beach, three miles away. The
existence of gold, however, on the coast from Nobby’s to
Tacking Point, was proved 20 years ago, but a reef has
never yet been hit on. Colours can he got in almost any
creek on the coast between Port Macquarie and Camden
Haven. # #
Since ’9O, Murchison Goldfields (W.A.) have
exported via Geraldton 36,6600 z. gold. Other estimates
make it 53,1600 z.

* * *
A grey-headed linen-draper gives a very bad
account of Ooolgardie. The practical miner in question
spent 35 years behind the counter of a large Melbourne
drapery establishment, and was then discharged by the
new firm. So he put his scissors in his pocket, together
with a tape for measuring off nuggets by the yard, and
set out to Coolgardie. It was a disgusting region, he
says. No trams or omnibuses passed the door, and as
for gold—he never saw a little bit. Fortunately, the old
fellow managed to get back, in a weak state, to Mel-
bourne, where his narrative has sent a shudder through
the soft-goods trade.
• * •
Since the Robinson Co. (South Africa) started,
it has crushed 300,000 tons, for over 20 tons of gold.
Though there are millions of tons of ore awaiting stamps,
the co. lately bought several adjoining blocks for
£120,000. They are estimated to contain over half a
million tons ore, which will yield £1,500,000 profit.
* # •
Mills United last fortnight: 1827 tons, 16760 z.
—a slight fall. Since starting it has crushed 87,559 tons
for 88,7650 z., and declared £123,750 divs. ; this half-year
is nearly £20,000 better than the last.

Shareholders, brokers, scrip-jobbers generally,
continue to watch developments in the Clarence mine,

The Red White and Blue, chiefly owned by
Lansell, has again entered the list of good yields and
steady divs. Last crushing, 414 ozs., div. 6d. Some
people were beginning to wonder whether the poor block
of ground would ever be taken out.
* * *
The latest opponent of the stamp-battery is
Carr’s disintegrator—two hollow wheels revolving against
each other and fitted with iron bars which act-as beaters.
It is claimed that hard stone can thus be reduced to
impalpable powder for less than Is. a ton. One of these
machines will be tested at Mt. Morgan, where Krome’s
rolls are larerely used for dry-crushing.

hydraulic mine, N.S.W., it should be said that work was
commenced on a basis of using 8,000,000 gallons a day,
and he protested against the reduction to about quarter
the quantity when so much money had been spent tc
complete the original scheme.

“Harry” M‘Quade, ex-M.P., and re-
nowned sportsman, died at Elizabeth Bay, Sydney,
of Bright’s disease, on Monday last, aged 48 years.
He was the eldest son of the late William
M'Quade, of Windsor (N.S.W.), and was a
popular property-owner in the Hawkesbury dis-
trict. He was M.P. for the electorate of that
name from ’B2 to ’B5, was one of the founders of
the Hawkesbury Coursing Club, was an enthusiast
in cricket, rowing and other manly sports, and
loved the stage, from which he married a most
charming and popular lady with whom and her
children a wide circle of friends will sincerely
condole. For “Harry” M'Quade was the very
soul of good-nature.
Sydney News item. More aldermen :
For Redfern two of the present members, Messrs. H.
C. Hoyle and J. S. M'Gowen, will oppose one another,
while Messrs. W. 0. Coombes, Cunningham, ex-
Alderman Ogden are already in the field, and, in
all probability, Alderman 0. Gordon (Mayor of the
borough) and Alderman Geo, Howe will also con-
test. For Waterloo, Aldermen Geo. Anderson and
E. T. Walsh (Waterloo), Messrs. G. W. Taylor, Mr. W. H.
Sharpe (one. of the present members), and Alderman
Horne (Alexandria) have themselves. At
Botany the contest will be a keen one in so much as there
are already three local candidates—viz., Mr. W. Stephen,
J.P., the Mayor of Botany (Alderman J. F. Luland),
and Alderman J. J. M'Fadyen, while Alderman J. Dacey,
of Alexandria, intends running in the labour-protection
interest. For the new electorate of Darlington Mr. W.
F. Schey, one of the present members, will face the music
with opponents in Alderman John Crowe (Redfern),
Alderman G. W. Lander (Redfern), Alderman Spring
(Mayor of Darlington), and the nominee of the local
Labour Electoral League.
Fourteen more alderman ! The Parliament of
Beadles is coming !
Mr. Cavanagh, father of the young lady
murdered at Carcoar by Glasson, called the other
day at The Bulletin office to deny the truth of
a paragraph to the effect that Mrs. Cavanagh, at
the instance of Mrs. Glasson, had signed a petition
for Glasson’s reprieve. Mr. Cavanagh added that
Mrs. Cavanagh had never even been asked to
sign such a petition. Since then we have received
the following;—
A statement appears in your last issue to the effect that
Mrs. Glasson called upon me and obtained my signature
to a petition for the reprieve of her husband. 1 beg to
give this statement an emphatic denial. I have never
seen Mrs. Glasson, and would not know her if I did see
her.—Yours truly, Letitia Cavanagh.
The Bulletin regrets its mis-statement, but it
printed the paragraph on what, at the time,
seemed good authority, and it did not assert that
Mrs. Glasson “personally called upon” Mrs.
Cavanagh. It also takes leave to indulge in another
regret—that the Law which in vain vengeance
hangs Glasson should also torture with a lifelong
crucifixion his innocent and helpless wife and yet-
unborn child. The hanging of the man is a
hideous and useless murder to pile upon the other
blood-deeds; but the deliberate social strangula-
tion of a woman under such circumstances, is a
horror from which the mind of civilised man
revolts.

Mb. A. Fraser Hill, who is lecturing throughout

people, and illustrating ? by means of a speaking
diagram, the glaring inequalities of wealth dis-
tribution, has submitted several long and interest-
ing manuscripts, from one of which a few
statements and deductions are extracted and here
presented.
According to Mr. Hill: If all the private wealth
and all the freehold land in New South Wales
were equally divided amongst all the men, each
man’s portion would be something like the fol-
lowing :
125 acres, freehold land, worth ... .£5OO
House and improvements, worth ... 300
Live stock and personal effects, worth 200
(Say) ... .£lOOO
And, in addition, he Would be entitled to lease
400 acres of pastoral land for <£4 per annum.
Why, then, should there be any poor and home-
less in N.S. Wales? Yet, Mr. Hill finds, by care-
fully cheeked processes of approved actuarial cal-
culation, estimating from the facts supplied by
probate returns, that the condition of the male
adults of N.S. Wales may be thus stated; —
Living in the eniovment of wealth... 100,406
Male adults without wealth ... ... 226,594
Which means that 3070 per cent, are in posses-
sion of all the wealth of the province, while 69’30
per cent are absolutely penniless, or, at least,
have no effects worth speaking about. Mr. Hill
estimates the total wealth held by private persons
in New South Wales at £327,200,000 all told
(Government Statistician Coghlan, in his u Wealth
and Progress,” ’92, places it much higher:—
£404,148,000.)
The accompanying diagram is a reduced photo*
graph of one prepared by Mr, Hill, in colours, and
will repay study. Working men, possessing
207 749 votes, are absolutely without possessions,
but live from hand to mouth ; while 862 other
persons own among them tHe unthinkable total
of £138,200,000. Similarly, 20,000,000 acres
df land are owned by fewer _ then than there
are minutes m a Working winters day { and if the
area that they cultivate Were to be represented
by one minute, the whole remaining period frond
dawn until sunset would be insufficient to syra-
bolise the vast areas they leave untouched ! They
do not occupy the land, but merely fence it round
with barbed wire and prevent others from occupy-
ing and using it. ,
In a democratic country, where th© peoples
votes make the laws, there are 2.) t ,320 voters
each without as much land as would make him a
grave, while a handful of other voters own
20,000,000 acres.

An Ounce of Fact is Worth a
Bushel of Theory.
In Life Assurance, as in most other things, the real test of the success of business done is
its stability.
Notwithstanding the fact that no travelling agent or travelling doctor has been out for the
Society for five years past, the records of the City Mutual Life for that period show its
position to be far and away the most satisfactory of any of the Australasian Life Offices estab-
lished during the past 25 years.
The Sydney Morning Herald of the 24th ult., reviewing the Life Assurance Census returns
recently furnished to the Government Statistician, printed a column of figures showing the pro-
portion of business retained by each of the colonial offices. The result showed that while the
average Australian Life Office has been able to keep 7 per cent, of its business, the C tty
Mutual Life, the youngest of the group, has kept 30 per cent., or over hour tunes as much.
The Agents of the City Mutual Life have always a good word to say about other
Australian Life Offices. The institution of Mutual Life Assurance in these colonies is a trium-
phant success, a gratifying result mainly due to the honourable and capable manner in which the
Life Offices have been conducted. But their own Society has been a long time coming to the
front it has got there at last ; it has already the reputation of being the Most Liberal and
Progressive Life Office in Australia, and they mean not only to sustain its popularity, but
increase it.

£ memorial of the old canteen system in
Indiai Has, lately been fodrid by a Pall Mall
correspondent. Epitaph in the cantomuenb
cemetery at Bangalore :
Sacred to the memory of John Wilson, private in His
Majesty’s 84th Regiment of Foot, who for the awful
crime of mutiny suffered death on 9lh Nov., 1815, with
the courage and fortitude of a Christian soldier.
The “awful crime” was caused hy the canteen regula-
tions then in force, under which every soldier was obliged
to pay for a dram of arrack every day, whether he
drank it or not! The 84th, lately arrived from England,
objected to the arrack arrangement, with the result that
two privates—John Wilson and Alfred Paget—were shot
as mutineers.
Gately (“ Ballaram ”), Victoria’s hanger
and flogger of 15 years ago, was naturally adapted
For his brutal business. He was a powerfully-
made animal, with a hoad and neck_ like a hull-
dog’s, and witli corresponding instincts. He
fexercised a delicate discrimination in the exercise;
of His craft, as the following complaint, made on a
post card to the then Governor of Melb. Gaol,
indicates:—
If an old convict is to be Executed, such as Weeeburch,
the hangman, Gately, arranges everything properly and
the culprit suffers nothing. If a man is to be executed
who doesn’t belong to the criminal class, such as Howard
or Duffus, Gately takes caire to arrange the rope in such a
manner that the poor wretch shall be slow ly strangled.
This demon, Gately, among a oert.ain class actually boasts
that he does this. This monster requires dismissal.
He didn’t get it, but Melbourne ultimately being
made too hot for him by larrikins, he resigned his
odious billet and went to Sydney, where ho
shortly after met a despicable death.

A Sydney whisky firm, after patiently
billing a small and hopeless customer without
getting any reply, at length threatened to “ take
proceedings.” The answer is another dismal com-
mentary on trade as it now is in Sydney
1 have received from your firm an intimation that, if I
do not pay inv whisky-hill,- you will place the matter in
the hands of your solicitor. The reason why 1 haven’t
paid the aforesaid bill is that I can’t .conscientiously
eficourage the nefarious traffic in drink. I regard whisky
as a curse; a destroying influence, and a cause of soul-
wreckage, a leprosy of the spirit, and a consuming fire in
the intestines. Alcohol In all its shapes is the trunk-line
that leads unto perdition, and there is a trail of empty
bottles along the road to Gehenna. Therefore I seriously
urge you not to sue me for that whisky-hill, for if I paid
It I would be perpetrating an out rage upon my conscience
and lending a hand to the unclean thing. “ Wine is a
mocker.” Further, if I sent you the cheque you ask for,
it would be no gdod, for the bank wouldn’t pay it at pre-
sent or any time this month, or even next month. In
fact, I don’t think they’d pay it even next year. Drink is
the parent of crime and the prolific cause of human
misery'. I had a vague idea of calling on you and trying to
negotiate for another ease, hut in the present strained
state of our relations I will have to postpone that pleasure.
1 thought of offering von a dictionary in settlement of
the account—the Imperial Dictionary, in four volumes,
costi‘6 69. l.iquor is the greatest evil with which this
country is afflicted. Can I offer you two Plymouth Kock
roosters in consideration of another month’s credit, or
can’t the matter he amicably settled in any way? The
only advantage about being sued is that it would give
your solicitor the pleasure of my acquaintance, hut I
don’t want to become acquainted with any more solicitors
—I know too many already. Alcohol is the embodiment
of sin and the incarnation of sorrow. You can have the
cheque any time you want it—provided you can lend me
a blank form, for my own cheque-book was finished some
weeks ago —hut what on earth would be the good of it to
you? And, in any case, do you—l ask yhti in confidence
as a square white man—do you happen to have the price
of adrink about you 7—Yours, &0., *

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A slight smile lit up the clear-cut, sombre
face of Lawsdn from Safune, as looking up from
bis boat at Etheridge’s house he saw the glint of
many lights shining through the Walls of the
roughly-built store. It was well on towards mid-
night when he had left Safune and sailed round to
Etiieridge’s, a distance of 12 or 15 miles, and as
his boat touched the sand the first streaks of
dawn were changing the dead whiteness of the
beach into a dull grey—soon to brighten into a
creamy yellow as the sun pierced the heavy land-
mist.
A native or two, wrapped from head to foot in
the long lava lava of white calico, passed him as
he followed the windings of the track to Ethe-
ridge’s, but gave him no signof greeting. Had he
been any one of the few other white men living
on Savaii the dark men would have stopped him
and, native-like, enquired the reason of Ills early
visit to their town. But they knew Lawson too
well. Mataaitu they called him — devil-faced. And
in this they were not far wrong, for Lawson, with
his dark olive skin, jet black beard, and eyes that
belied the ever-smiling lips, was not a man whom
people would he unanimous in trusting. The
natives knew him better than did liis few white
acquaintances in Samoa, for here, among them,
the mask that hid his inner nature from his com-
peers was sometimes put aside, though never
thrown away. But Etheridge, the hot-blooded
young Englishman and friend of six months’
standing, thought and spoke of him as “ the best
fellow in the world.”
# • #
Etheridge had been taking stock, and the
wearisome work had paled his usually florid
features. His face flushed with pleasure at
Lawson’s quiet voice :
“Hard at it, Etheridge? I don’t know which
looks the paler—you or Liolio. Why on earth
didn’t you send for me sooner? Anyone would
think you were some poor devil of a fellow trading
for the Dutchmen instead of being an indepen-
dent man. Now, I’m hungry and want breakfast
—that is, if Liolio isn’t too tired to get it ’’—and
he looked compassionately at Etheridge’s young
half-caste wife, sister to his own.
“I’m not tired,” said the girl, quietly. “I’ve
had easy tasks—counting packets of fish-hooks,
grosses of cotton, and things like that. Billy
wouldn’t let me help him with the prints and
heavy things ” —and with the faintest shadow of
a smile on her lips she passed through into the
sitting room and thence outside to the little
thatched cook-house a few yards away. With
ardent infatuation Etheridge rested his blue eyes
on the white-robed, slender figure as she stood at
the door and watched the NiuS cook light his fire
for an early cup of coffee—the first overture to
breakfast at Etiieridge’s.
* • »
“By Jove, Lawson, I’m the luckiest man
in Samoa to get such a wife as Liolio—and I only
a new-chum to the Islands. I believe she’d work
night and day if I’d allow it. And if it hadn’t
been for you I’d never have met her at all, but
would have married some fast creature who’d
have gone through me in a mouth and left me a
dead-broken beachcomber.”
“ Yes,” said Lawson, “she is a good girl, and,
except her sister, about the only half-caste I ever
knew whom I would trust implicitly. Their
mother was a Hervey Island woman, as i told you,
and Liolio has been with Terere and me all over
Polynesia, and I think I know her nature. She’s
fond of you, Etheridge, in her quiet, undemon-
strative way, but she’s a bit shy yet. You see,
you don’t speak either Rirotougan or Samoan,
and half-caste wives hate talking English. Now,
tell me, what is it worrying you? You haven’t
had another attack?”
“ Yes,” said the younger man, “ I have—and a
bad one, too, and that's why I sent for you. The
stock-taking is nothing ; but I was afraid I might
get another that would stiffen me properly. Look
here, Lawson, you’ve been a true friend to me.
You picked me up six months ago a drunken, half-
maddened beast in Apia and saved my life, reason
and money, and .”
“Bosh,” said Lawson, taking his coffee from
the hand of Etheridge’s wife; “don’t think of it,
my boy. Every man goes a bit crooked some-
times ; so don’t thank me too much,”
Etheridge waited lid his wife was gone and then
resumed: “I’ve been horribly scared, Lawson,
over this,” and he placed his hand over his heart,
“I was lifting a ease of bismits when I dropped
like a pithed bullock. When I came to, Liolio
was bathing my face. . . . I feel pretty shaky
still. The doctor at Goddeffroy’s warned me, too
—said I’d go off suddenly if I wasn’t careful. My
father and one brother died like that. And I
want to talk things over with you in case—you
know.”
Lawson nodded.
“Everything I have is for her, Lawson —land,
house, trade, and money. You’re pretty sure
there’s no irregularity in that will of mine, aren’t
you?”
“Sure. It’s very simply written. It’s properly
witnessed, and would hold in any court of law, if
contested. And perhaps your people in Australia
might do that.”
Eiheridge reddened. “No; I cut adrift from
’em long ago. Grog, you know. Beyond yourself
and Liolio, I haven’t a soul who’ll bother about
me. 1 think, Lawson, I’ll take a run up to Apia
and see the Dutch doctor again. Fearful cur, am
I not ?”
“ Come, Etheridge,” and Lawson laid his
smooth, shapely hand—how dishonest are shapely
hands ! —on the other’s arm. “ You’re a little
down. Anything wrong with one’s heart always
gives a man a bad shaking. There’s Liolio calling
us to breakfast, so I won’t say any more but this :
Even if Liolio wasn’t my wife’s sister, and any-
thing happened to you, there’s always a home for
her in my house. I’d do that for your sake alone,
old man, putting aside the principle I goon of
showing respect to any white man’s wife, even if
she were a Oahu girl and had rickety ideas of
morality.”
« # *
When Lawson had first met him and had
carried him down to his station on S tyaii, nursed
him through his illness, and treated him like a
brother, Etheridge, with the impulsive confidence
of his simple nature, poured out his thanks and
told his history ; and eagerly accepted Laws m’s
suggestion to try his hand at trading, instead of
continuing his erratic wanderings—wanderings
which could only end in his “going broke” at
Tahiti or Honolulu. Fifteen miles or so away,
Lawson said, the.re was a village with a good
opening for a trader. How -much could he put
intuit? Well, he had .£503 with him, and there
was another thousand in Sydney—the last of five.
Ample, said his host. So one day the land was
bought, a house and store put up, and Etheridge
commenced life as a trader.
Tlie strange tropic beauty of the place and the
ways of the people soon cast their spell over
Etheridge's imaginative nature, and he was as
happy as a man possibly could be —with a know-
ledge that his life hung by a th.rea 1. How
slender that thread was L iwsou knew, perhaps,
better than he. The German doctor had said :
“ You must dell him to he gareful, Mr. Lawson.
Any oxcideuumd, any zoode.n drouble mit any-
dings; dr too much visky midout any excide-
meuds, and he drop dead. I dell you.”
* * *
A month or so after he had settled,
Etheridge paid his weekly visit to Lawson, and
met Liolio. “This is my wife’s sister, ’ said
Lawson, “she has been on a visit to some friends
in Tutuila and came back in the Iserbrook."
The clear-cut, refined and beautiful features of
the girl did their work all too quickly on
Etheridge. He was not a sensualist, only a man
keenly susceptible to female beauty, and this girl
was beautiful —perhaps not so beautiful as her
sister, Terere, Lawson’s wife, but with a softer
and more tender light in her full, dark eyes. And
Lawson smiled to himself when Etheridge asked
him to come outside and smoke when his wife
and her sister had said good night. A
student of human nature, lie had long ago read
the simple mind of Etheridge as he would an
open book, and knew what was coming. They
went outside and talked —that is, Etheridge did.
Lawson listened and smoked. Then he put a
question to the other man.
* * *
“ Of coarse I will, Lawson ; do you think
I’m scoundrel enough to dream of anything else?
We’ll go up to Apia and get married by the white
missionary.”
Lawson laughed in his quiet way. “I wouldn t
think you a scoundrel at all, Etheridge. I may
as well tell you that I’m not married to her sister.
We negleoted doing that when I lived in the east-
ward groups, and uo one in Samoa is any the wiser
and wouldn’t think anything of it if they were.
But although I’m only a poor devil of a trader,
I’m a man of principle in some things, Liolio
is but a chi d, so to speak, and I’m her natural
protector. Now, you’re a fellow of some means
and if anything did happen to you she wouldn t
get a dollar if she wasn’t legally your wife. The
consul would claim everything until he heard
from your relatives. And she’s very young,
Etheridge, and you’ve told me often enough that
your heart’s pretty dicky. Dun t think me a
brute.” • (t-NT
Etheridge grasped his hand and wrung it. JNo,
no—a thousand times no. You’re the best-
hearted fellow in the world, and I honour you all
the more, Lawson. Will you ask her tomorrow ?”
Perhaps if he had heard the manner of Lawson’s
asking it would have puzzled his simple brain.
And the subdued merriment of the two sisters
might have caused him to wonder still more.
A week or so after, Etheridge and the two
sisters went up to Apia. Lawson was unable to
go. Copra was coming in freely, he had said with
a smile, and he was too poor to run away from
business—even to the wedding of his own wifes
sister.
# # #
As Etheridge and his young wife came out
of the mission church some natives and white
loafers stoodhvround and watched them.
“ Ho, Magalo,” said one, “ is not that teine, the
sister of the wife of Mataaitu, the black visaged
papa '(tfji ?”
“ Aye,” answered a skinny old hag, carrying a
basket of wat'-r-bot'tles, “-’tie she, and the other
is Terere. I lived with them once at J utnila. fslio
who is now made a wife and looketh so good arid
holy went away but a year ago with {.lie captain
of a ship—a pig of a German —and now, look you,
she marrieth an Englishman.’’
The other natives laughed, and then an ugly
fat-faced girl with lime-covered head in id painter!
cheeks called out “ L’apati tele ! ” and 1 ere re
turned round and cursed them in good English.
“ What does that mean 1 ’’ said a white man to
Flash Harry from Saleimoa —apian lull of island
lore. . 1-
“Why, it means as the bride isn t alius she
purfesses to bo. Them pretty soft-lookin’ ones,
like her seldom is, in Samoa,or any where else. ” -
** * ’
The day following the stock-taking
Etheridge went to Apia—and never came back.
One night a native tapped geuty at‘Lawson's “
window and handed him a note. As he read
Terere with a sleepy yawn awoke, and, stretching:
one rounded arm out at full length, let it fall
lazily on the mat-bed.
“ What is it, Jlarry.”
“Get up, d you ! Etheridge is dead, and
I’m going to take Liolio up to Apia as quick as !■
can. Why the h— couldn’t he die here ? ”
A rapid vision of unlimited presents from the
rich young widow passed through the mind of
Terere —to whom the relations that had formerly
existed between her and Lawson were well known
—as she and he sped along in his boat to
Etheridge’s, Liolio received the news with much
equanimity and a few tears, and then leaving
Terere in charge she got into the boat and rolled
a cigarette. Lawson was in feverish baste. He
was afraid the consul would be down and baulk
his rapid but carefully arranged scheme. At
Safune, he sent his crew of two men ashore to his
house for a breaker of water, and then once they '
were out of sight lie pushed off and I ft them.-
They were in the way and might spoil every thing.
The breeze was strong, and that night Lawson
and Liolio, instead of lining out in the open sea
beating up to Apia, were ashore in; the sitting-
room of the white missionary house on the other
side of Savair.
# # *
“I am indeed glad to make your acquaint-
ance, Mr. Lawson. Your honourable impulse
deserves commendation. I have always regretted
the fact that annul like you whose reputation as.
an educated and intelligent person far above that
of most traders here is not unknown to me (Law-
son smiled sweetly) should not alone set at defiance
the teaching of Holy Writ hut tacitly irnnkatnar
efforts to inculcate a higher code of morality in
these beautiful islands. Ere long I trust I may
make the acquaintance of your brother-in-law, Mr.
Etheridge, and his wife.”
Lawson smiled affably and a slight tinge suffused
the creamy cheek of Liolio.
“ And now, Mr. Lawson, as you are so very
anxious to get back home I will not delay. Here

• • •
“Get in, you little beast,” said Lawson, as lie
bundled Liolio into the boat and started back
home, “and don’t fall overboard. I don’t want
to lose the Best Asset in that Fool’s Estate.”
• • •
When the consul, a week later* came down
to take possession of Etheridge’s “estate,” he
called in at Safnne to ask Lawson to come and help
him to take an inventory. Terere met him with
a languid smile, and, too lazy perhaps to speak
English, answered his questions in Samoan.
“ He’s married and gone,” she said. “ Married?
Aren’t you, Mrs. Lawson ?” said the bewildered
consul, in English.
“Not now, sir, my sister is. Will you take me
to Apia in your boat, please ?”
• • #
And that is how Lawson, the papalayi
mativa (poor white) and “the best-hearted fellow
in the world,” became a man aloa —a man of
riches, and went, with the Best Asset in
Etheridge’s estate, the calm-eyed Liolio, to start
a hotel in—well, no matter where.
LOUIS BECKE.
Dear Bulletin, —Be your par. from
“ Astruc,” I would remark that if Jesus of Naza-
reth was not a working man, His contemporary
biographers have wonderfully misrepresented, and
18J centuries have strangely misconceived Him.
Amongst a people bi-lingual of necessity, and
whose ancestral language was still cherished, the
mastery of three languages is by no means evi-
dence of a youth exempt from labour. The mere
mention of the subject suggests the name of Elihu
Burritt. And the germ of Talmudic lore, which
existed in Christ’s time, was no food for a life-
time’s study, when, as in His case, it was known
only to be contemned and repudiated as the thing
“said by them of old time,” or the “ traditions ”
which stultified the word of God. The Talmud
(Palestinian) of after generations was, in Christ’s
time, known as the “oral,”in contradistinction to
the “ written,” law, and was never put into
writing until—according to a tradition more or
less unreliable, though doubtless containing a
kernel of truth—only two Jews who knew the
oral lawsurvived the suppression of the rising under
Baroocheba, 130 A.D. The comparison of Christ’s
sympathy for the people with that of the late Chief
Justice of Victoria is simply absurd. Higin-
botham was a democrat by sentiment, but his
aloofness brings him into sharp contrast with
Christ, the democrat per se, who was not only
the champion of the poor, but their veritable
comrade. Higinbotham, clean - handed, clear-
headed, and generous-hearted, was yet the product
and victim of caste. Even his historic phrase,
“the wealthy lower orders,” when subjected to
close analysis, reeks with unconscious exclusive-
ness. His sympathy with the people has alwayr
been appreciated and honoured, but what army of
followers recognised in him the superior spirit of
their own order? If we might compare the
immeasurable with the measurable, and if the
comparison with a living man were not prema-
ture, we might rather say that the sympathy of
Christ resembled that of John Burns, the artisan.
This in full view of the possibility that Burns
may yet sheathe his unbroken steel, as the
fastidious Higinbotham did 20 years ago.— Tom
Collins.
“My One Exception.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I heartily encore the sentiment that the
collective Mongolian is the “Yellow Curse” of
Australia. But listen to the tale Of “My One
Exception.”
* • #
In the Western district, some ten years
back, I was rounding up a frisky steer that had
broken away from my mob. My horse; in trying
to manoeuvre a stumpy grass-tree, stumbled and
somersaulted, landing me somewhere in the
vioinity. I tried to rise, but couldn’t! My left
leg was broken just above the ankle; my head
bruised and bleeding. Presently L swooned.
When my senses returned, a face met mine; It
belonged to a Chinese hawker whom I had passed
on the homestead track an hour before, with a
simple “Good morning, John.” Somehow or
other, the bush man’s usual gory greetings to the
Chow-pedlar had been forgotten by me that morn-
ing, and my brief salutation must have sounded
quite friendly.
My exact position when I came to was interest-
ing. I was sitting up in one of John’s baskets
(which he had half emptied of its contents) with
my broken limb (very neatly splinted, by the way)
resting on a couple of straight sticks tied across
the top of the basket. My back was supported
by two upright twigs stuck down amongst the
goods, and to complete my rig-out, the Eastern
man’s sunshade was fastened over me, so that I
was thoroughly sheltered from the sun—which
was red hot! The whole thing nonplussed me;
the arrangements would have done credit to an
ambulance prize-winner. Only for the pain-
cursed limb and the aching and dizziness about
the head, I’d have sworn that I was d d com-
fortable.
A smile —like a play of limelight on a dirty
bladder lit up the Mongolian’s face 1 “ All
lightee; keepee quiet! me cally you home,” he
murmured, soothingly, and, in a twinkling, I was
swinging in one of the baskets, at the end of his
bamboo, and moving in the direction of the home-
stead ! That journey was a 10-miler, over a
bumpy bush-track, with a temp, of 110 in the
shade. My rescuer rolled in perspiration, and
panted under his heavy load, witli no other re-
compense than an occasional faint, but sincere,
“ You’re a white man.”
After four hours, John landed me safely
at the station, and the boys rushed me in
and began doctoring. In the excitement of at-
tending on me, my Mongolian friend was tem-
porarily forgotten, and when they looked for him
he had gone. VVe all thought he had only jour-
neyed back to pick up the goods left ou the plain,
and that he would return to the station, but we
were wrong. VVe never ran across him again.
Melbourne.

A candidly Tory specimeri of a British
colonel has given his opinion upon the rapid Royal
promotions of the Duke of Connaught, Whose army
“seniority ” proceeds on ago-as-the-Queen-pleases
principle. Said the colonel*:—“There was no
doubt that the Duke had had exceptional oppor-
tunities; but it was not much use being born under
fexceptional circilmstanceS if one did not have ex-
ceptional opportunities.” This reasoning applies
jdst as convincingly to men of all grades in all
Walks df life. What is the use of being born of
beggar parents if you are not to enjoy suitable op-
portunities of dying in the gutter?
Another liew society has just been started.
Bristol is its headquarters ; the Duke of Beaufort
is its president. Its title is the Property Protec-
tion Society. Most people are under the im-
pression that property is sufficiently protected
already in a country where a man is sometimes
find 20s. for kicking his wife about the head, and
is sent to prison for six months for stealing a
bunch of turnips. But they are greatly mistaken.
An amendment of the law is needed, so that any-
one trespassing on enclosed land and annexing any
product of the soil shall be punishable by justices
sitting in Petty Sessions. — Echo.
The mild Hindoo is a good deal more
candid upon his census paper than the average
European. Unobtrusively frank list of occupa-
tions, supplied at last Indian census :
Marriage-arranger, women’s barber, sorcerer, planet
worshipper, living upon hidden wealth, player on the
tom-tom, player on the round drum, helping people to
put on their clothes, making bedstead legs, servant for
life, beggar giving his blessing, taking fees for preaching
and reciting, guardian of minor, well-wisher of the public,
going to Hindustan, visitor, infant on milk, living happily,
idler, gambler, thief, daooit, living on loans, living on son-
in-law, giving daughters in marriage for money, rupees
five a month from stepson, player on four-wired instru-
ments, oil rubber, guest inviter, umbrella-carrier, buffalo
barber, storm and hail averter, giving oracles, taking
offerings on the birth of a son, servant to a candidate,
buying and eating bread, trashseller, honorary magistrate,
orphan, traveller, guest, making presents, witness in
court for wages, hanger-on, talebearer from house to
house, village thief, debtor, bagging from relations,
inheritance from son-in-law.
The value of a woman in brutal England,
the country which sends out its snufflebusters to
the heathen all over the earth :
John Lennox was charged at Birmingham with violently
assaulting his wife. In consequence of his violence the
woman had to leave the accused. On Saturday night he
encountered her in Hill street. She was on the opposite
side of the road, and he rushed across, and, attacking her
from behind, felled her to the ground. He then kicked
her savagely about the head. He inflicted a terrible gash
on the jaw, and knocked two of her teeth out. There
were eight previous convictions recorded against the
accused, and he was fined 20s. and costs, in default one
month’s imprisonment.
Knocked down, kicked brutally on the head, jaw
gashed, two teeth kicked out, eight previous con-
victions —twenty shillings. Then the next case
concerned a starving woman (with no previous
convictions), who stole three pairs of boots and
sold them to get food for her children—one of
whom was dying. She got six weeks’ gaol with
hard labour to vindicate the sacred rights of Pro-
perty.
A Decision.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The girl was in a quandary. There they stood,
the pair of them, with their heads uncovered, a
digger and a farmer —rival suitors for her hand.
They wanted the matter settled. As for looks,
she was a tolerable Australienne, and in a matter-
of-fact way she called upon them to state their
cases promptly.
The digger spoke first. “D’ye see, Miss,” said
he, “ here’s my heart. I get a bit of gold these
times” (his hat was twirling furiously now), “and
I want you. I drinks, I swears, I smokes, but—l
can’t get no further. I’m kind o’ fair bam-
foozled.”
Then the farmer belched out his emotions (sen-
sations is a better word). He had land. He
wanted a wife bad to hold things together a bit.
She would be useful to him. There was a sphere
ahead of her. She could steer the show to profit-
able results.
The girl dallied awhile. No judge of the
weightiest law point ever had a greater dilemma
to shunt. The weighing-scales were lop-sided.
The rays of the setting sun pushed themselves
through the bushes and played in fairy circles
about her head as she stood —nervous, yet motion-
less. She seemed poised, even as the chamois ere
its springs beyond the chasm.
At last, the girl said tersely—
“ Behold, I chuck myself away. You drink
you swear, you smoke, but—that is no sign of a
bad heart. I have a right to dispose of myself. I
will take you‘for it, because I love you. I will
gratify my love were I to blast my life a million
times.”
And with a naivete (result of inexperience) that
drove the farmer almost crazy, she tumbled into
the miner’s arms.
And then they departed on their several ways—
the happy pair to spend their honeymoon in a
calico tent, and the farmer to the warden’s court
to apply for an injunction, praying that his
rival be compelled to suspend mining opera-
tions in the creek, as the silt was sending the tail-
ings and the stream fast upon his land.

LADIES!
Your attention, please, to the Fashions which
bloom in the Spring.
MARK FOY’S will prove a garden of pleasure during
the coming season. Ladies will find a refreshing delight
in a daily visit to FOY’S, where the glittering sprays of
fashionable gems from the continental artists of fashion are
being opened up with all speed.
■■-.>•.. ■ ■ , \ '
Great is the curiosity' and joy of young and old, to
see and admire the spick-and-span novelties from Paris,
London, and elsewhere. v
MILLINERY, in wonderfully grotesque shapes and
extraordinary colourings, are more varied than the wildest’
flights of imagination could conceive.
